The Hanafi Ruling on al-'Eena: Permissible or Disliked?
Hanafi ruling on 'eena and intra-madhhab reconciliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'eena, distinguish when it is disliked versus permissible, and explain the legal reasoning behind the positions of Abu Yusuf and Ibn al-Humam.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school permits 'eena as a sale, but dislikes it if the same asset returns to the seller.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position on a controversial transaction called 'eena, where a seller extends credit at a higher price and then buys back the item for cash. Abu Yusuf famously declared it permissible and rewarded, while Ibn al-Humam later reconciled conflicting reports by adding a condition of dislikedness when the asset returns. Understanding this nuance is key to applying sadd al-dhara'i' correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and its ruling
Read the passage and determine what 'eena is and what ruling Abu Yusuf gives. Note that he says it is 'jā'izah ma’jūrah' (permissible and rewarded).
Rewarded because it avoids outright riba.
2
Recognize the apparent contradiction
Other Hanafi texts report dislikedness (karāhah). Ibn al-Humam attempts to reconcile: he notes that when the same asset returns to the seller, it is makrūh; otherwise it is only khilāf al-awlā.
This reconciliation preserves both rulings.
3
Examine the reasoning for permissibility
Abu Yusuf's justification: the seller flees from the forbidden (riba) by using a sale. The credit term corresponds to a portion of the price; the lender is not obliged to give a loan.
This is an application of istiḥsān.
4
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Ibn al-Humam gives an example: a needy person wants a loan, but the lender sells a good on credit; the buyer then sells it for cash. This is permissible because the asset does not return to the seller.
The ruling depends on the specific facts.
5
Compare with the opposing view
The Maliki school views this as a prohibited means to usury (blocked by sadd al-dhara'i'). Note that the Hanafi response is that this is a disputed matter.
This ikhtilāf is discussed in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبويوسف رحمه الله تعالى: "العِينة جائزة مأجورة" وقال: "أجره لمكان الفرار عن الحرام." وحاول ابن الهمام رحمه الله تعالى أن يُطبّق بين القولين، فقال: "ثمّ الذى يقع فى قلبى أنَّ ما يُخرجه الدافع إن فعلت صورة يعود فيها إليه هو أو بعضه، كعود الثّوب أو الحرير فى الصورة الأولى (۱)، وكَعَودِ العشرة في صورة إقراض الخمسة عشر" فمكروة، وإلا فلا كراهة إلا خلاف الأولى على بعض الاحتمالات، كأن يحتاج المديون فيأبى المسئول أن يُقرض، بل أن يبيع ما يُساوى عشرة بخمسة عشر إلى أجل، فيشتريه المديون ويبيعه فى السوق بعشرة حالة، ولا بأس في هذا، فإنّ الأجل قابله قِسطُ من الثّمن، والقرض غير واجب عليه دائماً، بل هو مندوب، فإن تركه بمجرد رغبة عنه إلى زيادة الدنيا فمكروة، أو لعارض يُعذر به فلا، وإنّما يُعرف ذلك في خصوصيات المواد، وما لم ترجع إليه العين التي خرجت منه لا يُسمّى ببيع العينة. (١)
ولقد حقق الإمام الشاطبي رحمه الله تعالى مسئلة سد الذرائع في مثل هذه المجتهدات بكلام متين، نحكى بعضه لما فيه من فوائد قال رحمه الله تعالى: "فإن الذرائع على ثلاثة أقسام: منها ما يُسَدُّ باتفاق؛ كسب الأصنام مع العلم بأنه مؤد إلى سب الله تعالى، وكَسَبُ أَبَوَى الرجل إذا كان مؤديا إلى سب أبوي الساب؛ فإنه عُد في الحديث سبا من الساب لأبَوَى نفسه، وحفر الآبار فى طرق المسلمين مع العلم بوقوعهم فيها، وإلقاء السَّم في الأطعمة والأشربة التي يُعلم تناول المسلمين لها. ومنها ما لا يُسَدُّ باتفاق، كما إذا أحب الإنسان أن يشترى بطعامه أفضل منه أو أدنى من جنسه؛ فيتحيل ببيع متاعه ليتوصل بالثّمن إلى مقصوده، بل كسائر التجارات؛ فإن مقصودها الذي أبيحت له إنّما يرجعُ إلى التحيل في بَدْلِ دراهم في السلعة ليأخذ أكثر منها. ومنها ما هو مختلف فيه، ومسألتنا من هذا القسم؛ فلم نخرج عن حكمه بعد، والمنازعة باقية فيه. وهذه جملة ما يمكن أن يُقال في الاستدلال على جواز التحيل فى المسألة، وأدلة الجهة الأخرى (۲) مقرّرة واضحة شهيرة؛ فطالعها في مواضعها، وإنّما قصد هنا هذا التقرير الغريب لقلة الاطلاع عليه كتب أهله؛ ( إذ كتب الحنفيّة كالمعدومة الوجود في بلاد المغرب وكذلك كتب الشافعية وغيرهم من أهل المذاهب، ومع أن اعتياد الاستدلال لمذهب واحدٍ ربما يكسب الطالب نُفورًا وإنكارًا لمذهب غير مذهبه من غیر اطلاع على مأخذه؛ فيُورِثُ ذلك حَزَارَةً في الاعتقاد في الأئمة، الذين أجمع النّاسُ على فضلهم وتقدمهم في الدين، واضطلاعهم بمقاصد الشارع وفهم أغراضه، وقد وجد هذا كثيراً. (۳)
(۱) يعني إذا باع ثوباً بأكثر من ثمن السوق مؤجلا، ثم اشتراه منه بسعر السوق الذي هو أقل حالاً. (۲) يعني الجهة المانعة، مثل المالكية. (۳) الموافقات، كتاب المقاصد، القسم الثاني: مقاصد المكلف ۲: ۳۹۰ و ۳۹۱
Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'The 'eena (sale on credit with repurchase) is permissible and rewarded.' He said: 'I reward him because of his fleeing from the forbidden.' Ibn al-Humam, may Allah have mercy on him, tried to reconcile between the two statements, saying: 'Then what occurs to my heart is that what the giver gives out if a form occurs in which it or some of it returns to him, like the return of the garment or silk in the first picture, and like the return of ten in the picture of lending fifteen, it is disliked (makruh), otherwise there is no dislike except contrary to what is better (khilaf al-awla) on some probabilities, as when the debtor needs and the respondent refuses to lend, rather he sells what is worth ten for fifteen on credit, so the debtor buys it and sells it in the market for ten cash, and there is nothing wrong with this, for the credit corresponds to a portion of the price, and lending is not obligatory upon him always, rather it is recommended; if he leaves it merely out of desire for worldly increase, it is disliked, or for an excusable reason then not. This is known only in particular specific cases. And what does not have the original asset return to him is not called 'eena sale. (1)
And Imam al-Shatibi, may Allah have mercy on him, thoroughly investigated the issue of blocking the means (sadd al-dhara'i') in such ijtihad matters with solid speech; we will narrate some of it for its benefits. He said: 'The means are of three types: those that are blocked by consensus, like idol-making with knowledge that it leads to cursing Allah, and cursing a man's parents if it leads to cursing the curser's own parents, for it is considered in the hadith as cursing one's own parents, and digging wells in Muslim roads with knowledge that they will fall into them, and throwing poison into foods and drinks that Muslims are known to consume. Among them are those that are not blocked by consensus, as when a person wants to buy with his food something better or inferior of its kind; so he contrives by selling his goods to reach his goal through the price, indeed like all trades; for their purpose for which they were permitted is only to contrive in exchanging dirhams for a commodity to take more than that. And among them are those that are differed over, and our issue is of this type; we have not yet come out of its ruling, and the dispute remains. This is a summary of what can be said in arguing for the permissibility of contrivance in the issue, and the proofs of the other side (2) are established, clear, and well-known; consult them in their places. My aim here is only this unfamiliar presentation due to the lack of access to the books of its people; for Hanafi books are nearly non-existent in the Maghreb, as well as Shafi'i and other madhhabs. And the habit of arguing for one madhhab may cause the student aversion and denial of other madhhabs without knowledge of their bases; this produces harshness in belief concerning the imams whom all people agree upon their virtue and precedence in religion and their grasp of the purposes of the Lawgiver and understanding of His aims, and this has been found much.' (3)
(1) Meaning: when he sells a garment for more than market price on credit, then buys it from him at market price which is less cash. (2) Meaning: the prohibiting side, like the Maliki school. (3) Al-Muwafaqat, Book of Purposes, Second Section: Purposes of the Obligated, 2:390-391.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal status of 'eena (a sale with a prearranged repurchase at a lower cash price, effectively a loan with interest), and then expands into the broader usul principle of sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking the means to evil). It begins by citing Abu Yusuf's permissive view, that 'eena is permissible and even rewarded because it is a means of avoiding outright usury. Ibn al-Humam attempts to reconcile between different statements by distinguishing cases: if the same item returns to the original seller, it is makruh (disliked); otherwise it is merely khilaf al-awla (contrary to better), unless there is a genuine need. He gives the example of a needy person who wants a loan; if the lender refuses to lend but instead sells something on credit, and the buyer then sells it for cash, there is nothing wrong because the credit term corresponds to a portion of the price, and the lender is not obligated to give a loan. The ruling depends on the specifics.

Then al-Shatibi's analysis of sadd al-dhara'i' is quoted. He divides means into three categories: (1) those that are blocked by consensus because they almost always lead to harm (e.g., idol-making, cursing parents, digging wells in the path, poisoning food); (2) those that are not blocked by consensus because they are inherently permissible or beneficial (e.g., trade and ordinary contracts, which involve seeking profit but are essential); (3) those that are disputed, and the issue of 'eena falls into this category. Al-Shatibi, being Maliki, notes that the Maliki school tends to block such means, but he acknowledges that other schools (like Hanafis) have valid proofs. He warns against fanaticism and ignorance of other madhhabs, which leads to harboring ill feelings towards esteemed imams. The passage serves as a lesson in both the substantive ruling and the methodology of respecting ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
العينة (al-'eena): A sale where the buyer purchases goods on credit at a higher price and then immediately sells them back to the seller for cash at a lower price, effectively a loan with interest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Humam, when is 'eena considered disliked (makruh)?
A) Always, because it is a form of riba
B) When the same asset returns to the original seller
C) When the credit period is too long
D) When the buyer and seller are related

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits 'eena as a genuine sale, but dislikes it when the same asset returns to the seller, reconciling the permissive and restrictive narrations.

Al-Shatibi's Three Categories of Sadd al-Dhara'i'
Usul al-fiqh analysis of sadd al-dhara'i' categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify al-Shatibi's three categories of means that are blocked, not blocked, or disputed, and classify specific examples including 'eena.

Core Idea
Means that lead to evil are blocked by consensus, permissible by consensus, or disputed; 'eena belongs to the disputed category.

Imam al-Shatibi, a Maliki usuli, provides a clear categorization of the principle of blocking means (sadd al-dhara'i'), dividing them into three types. This lesson examines his classification and applies it to the case of 'eena, showing how ikhtilaf plays out in usul al-fiqh. The passage also offers a valuable lesson in scholarly humility and avoiding fanaticism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Read al-Shatibi's statement and list the three types: those blocked by consensus, those not blocked by consensus, and those differed over.
Memorize the categories.
2
Examine examples for the first category
Examples: idol-making, cursing parents, digging wells in Muslim roads, poisoning food. These are blocked because they almost certainly lead to harm.
These are clear cases of means leading to evil.
3
Examine examples for the second category
Examples: selling goods to obtain a better item of the same kind, and all trade. These are not blocked because they are beneficial and the means are not inherently harmful.
Trade involves seeking profit but is essential.
4
Classify 'eena into the third category
Al-Shatibi explicitly says 'eena is from the disputed category. Understand why: it is a means that could lead to riba but also avoids it.
This reflects the Hanafi-Maliki ikhtilaf.
5
Reflect on the adab (etiquette) lesson
Al-Shatibi warns against fanaticism and ignorance of other madhhabs. He notes that Hanafi books were scarce in the Maghreb, leading to unfair prejudice.
A mufti must respect all mujtahid imams.

وقال أبويوسف رحمه الله تعالى: "العِينة جائزة مأجورة" وقال: "أجره لمكان الفرار عن الحرام." وحاول ابن الهمام رحمه الله تعالى أن يُطبّق بين القولين، فقال: "ثمّ الذى يقع فى قلبى أنَّ ما يُخرجه الدافع إن فعلت صورة يعود فيها إليه هو أو بعضه، كعود الثّوب أو الحرير فى الصورة الأولى (۱)، وكَعَودِ العشرة في صورة إقراض الخمسة عشر" فمكروة، وإلا فلا كراهة إلا خلاف الأولى على بعض الاحتمالات، كأن يحتاج المديون فيأبى المسئول أن يُقرض، بل أن يبيع ما يُساوى عشرة بخمسة عشر إلى أجل، فيشتريه المديون ويبيعه فى السوق بعشرة حالة، ولا بأس في هذا، فإنّ الأجل قابله قِسطُ من الثّمن، والقرض غير واجب عليه دائماً، بل هو مندوب، فإن تركه بمجرد رغبة عنه إلى زيادة الدنيا فمكروة، أو لعارض يُعذر به فلا، وإنّما يُعرف ذلك في خصوصيات المواد، وما لم ترجع إليه العين التي خرجت منه لا يُسمّى ببيع العينة. (١)
ولقد حقق الإمام الشاطبي رحمه الله تعالى مسئلة سد الذرائع في مثل هذه المجتهدات بكلام متين، نحكى بعضه لما فيه من فوائد قال رحمه الله تعالى: "فإن الذرائع على ثلاثة أقسام: منها ما يُسَدُّ باتفاق؛ كسب الأصنام مع العلم بأنه مؤد إلى سب الله تعالى، وكَسَبُ أَبَوَى الرجل إذا كان مؤديا إلى سب أبوي الساب؛ فإنه عُد في الحديث سبا من الساب لأبَوَى نفسه، وحفر الآبار فى طرق المسلمين مع العلم بوقوعهم فيها، وإلقاء السَّم في الأطعمة والأشربة التي يُعلم تناول المسلمين لها. ومنها ما لا يُسَدُّ باتفاق، كما إذا أحب الإنسان أن يشترى بطعامه أفضل منه أو أدنى من جنسه؛ فيتحيل ببيع متاعه ليتوصل بالثّمن إلى مقصوده، بل كسائر التجارات؛ فإن مقصودها الذي أبيحت له إنّما يرجعُ إلى التحيل في بَدْلِ دراهم في السلعة ليأخذ أكثر منها. ومنها ما هو مختلف فيه، ومسألتنا من هذا القسم؛ فلم نخرج عن حكمه بعد، والمنازعة باقية فيه. وهذه جملة ما يمكن أن يُقال في الاستدلال على جواز التحيل فى المسألة، وأدلة الجهة الأخرى (۲) مقرّرة واضحة شهيرة؛ فطالعها في مواضعها، وإنّما قصد هنا هذا التقرير الغريب لقلة الاطلاع عليه كتب أهله؛ ( إذ كتب الحنفيّة كالمعدومة الوجود في بلاد المغرب وكذلك كتب الشافعية وغيرهم من أهل المذاهب، ومع أن اعتياد الاستدلال لمذهب واحدٍ ربما يكسب الطالب نُفورًا وإنكارًا لمذهب غير مذهبه من غیر اطلاع على مأخذه؛ فيُورِثُ ذلك حَزَارَةً في الاعتقاد في الأئمة، الذين أجمع النّاسُ على فضلهم وتقدمهم في الدين، واضطلاعهم بمقاصد الشارع وفهم أغراضه، وقد وجد هذا كثيراً. (۳)
(۱) يعني إذا باع ثوباً بأكثر من ثمن السوق مؤجلا، ثم اشتراه منه بسعر السوق الذي هو أقل حالاً. (۲) يعني الجهة المانعة، مثل المالكية. (۳) الموافقات، كتاب المقاصد، القسم الثاني: مقاصد المكلف ۲: ۳۹۰ و ۳۹۱
Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'The 'eena (sale on credit with repurchase) is permissible and rewarded.' He said: 'I reward him because of his fleeing from the forbidden.' Ibn al-Humam, may Allah have mercy on him, tried to reconcile between the two statements, saying: 'Then what occurs to my heart is that what the giver gives out if a form occurs in which it or some of it returns to him, like the return of the garment or silk in the first picture, and like the return of ten in the picture of lending fifteen, it is disliked (makruh), otherwise there is no dislike except contrary to what is better (khilaf al-awla) on some probabilities, as when the debtor needs and the respondent refuses to lend, rather he sells what is worth ten for fifteen on credit, so the debtor buys it and sells it in the market for ten cash, and there is nothing wrong with this, for the credit corresponds to a portion of the price, and lending is not obligatory upon him always, rather it is recommended; if he leaves it merely out of desire for worldly increase, it is disliked, or for an excusable reason then not. This is known only in particular specific cases. And what does not have the original asset return to him is not called 'eena sale. (1)
And Imam al-Shatibi, may Allah have mercy on him, thoroughly investigated the issue of blocking the means (sadd al-dhara'i') in such ijtihad matters with solid speech; we will narrate some of it for its benefits. He said: 'The means are of three types: those that are blocked by consensus, like idol-making with knowledge that it leads to cursing Allah, and cursing a man's parents if it leads to cursing the curser's own parents, for it is considered in the hadith as cursing one's own parents, and digging wells in Muslim roads with knowledge that they will fall into them, and throwing poison into foods and drinks that Muslims are known to consume. Among them are those that are not blocked by consensus, as when a person wants to buy with his food something better or inferior of its kind; so he contrives by selling his goods to reach his goal through the price, indeed like all trades; for their purpose for which they were permitted is only to contrive in exchanging dirhams for a commodity to take more than that. And among them are those that are differed over, and our issue is of this type; we have not yet come out of its ruling, and the dispute remains. This is a summary of what can be said in arguing for the permissibility of contrivance in the issue, and the proofs of the other side (2) are established, clear, and well-known; consult them in their places. My aim here is only this unfamiliar presentation due to the lack of access to the books of its people; for Hanafi books are nearly non-existent in the Maghreb, as well as Shafi'i and other madhhabs. And the habit of arguing for one madhhab may cause the student aversion and denial of other madhhabs without knowledge of their bases; this produces harshness in belief concerning the imams whom all people agree upon their virtue and precedence in religion and their grasp of the purposes of the Lawgiver and understanding of His aims, and this has been found much.' (3)
(1) Meaning: when he sells a garment for more than market price on credit, then buys it from him at market price which is less cash. (2) Meaning: the prohibiting side, like the Maliki school. (3) Al-Muwafaqat, Book of Purposes, Second Section: Purposes of the Obligated, 2:390-391.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal status of 'eena (a sale with a prearranged repurchase at a lower cash price, effectively a loan with interest), and then expands into the broader usul principle of sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking the means to evil). It begins by citing Abu Yusuf's permissive view, that 'eena is permissible and even rewarded because it is a means of avoiding outright usury. Ibn al-Humam attempts to reconcile between different statements by distinguishing cases: if the same item returns to the original seller, it is makruh (disliked); otherwise it is merely khilaf al-awla (contrary to better), unless there is a genuine need. He gives the example of a needy person who wants a loan; if the lender refuses to lend but instead sells something on credit, and the buyer then sells it for cash, there is nothing wrong because the credit term corresponds to a portion of the price, and the lender is not obligated to give a loan. The ruling depends on the specifics.

Then al-Shatibi's analysis of sadd al-dhara'i' is quoted. He divides means into three categories: (1) those that are blocked by consensus because they almost always lead to harm (e.g., idol-making, cursing parents, digging wells in the path, poisoning food); (2) those that are not blocked by consensus because they are inherently permissible or beneficial (e.g., trade and ordinary contracts, which involve seeking profit but are essential); (3) those that are disputed, and the issue of 'eena falls into this category. Al-Shatibi, being Maliki, notes that the Maliki school tends to block such means, but he acknowledges that other schools (like Hanafis) have valid proofs. He warns against fanaticism and ignorance of other madhhabs, which leads to harboring ill feelings towards esteemed imams. The passage serves as a lesson in both the substantive ruling and the methodology of respecting ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
سد الذرائع (sadd al-dhara'i'): The principle of blocking the means that lead to evil, categorized into three types: blocked by consensus, not blocked by consensus, and disputed.
العينة (al-'eena): A sale where the buyer purchases goods on credit at a higher price and then immediately sells them back to the seller for cash at a lower price, effectively a loan with interest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Shatibi, which of the following actions falls into the category that is disputed (mukhtalaf fihi)?
A) Idol-making
B) 'Eena transaction
C) Selling food to buy better food
D) Digging a well on a Muslim road

Key Takeaway
Al-Shatibi's three-category division of sadd al-dhara'i' provides a nuanced framework: some means are clearly blocked, some are clearly not, and some require ijtihad, like 'eena.

When Ifta Becomes Obligatory: Fard Kifaya and Fard 'Ayn
Explaining the default and exceptional obligations of ifta

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when ifta becomes fard 'ayn versus fard kifaya and apply the criteria to practical scenarios.

Core Idea
Ifta is communal by default; individual obligation arises only when you are the sole qualified person, when delay causes harm, or when officially appointed.

This lesson clarifies the foundational ruling for every mufti: when is issuing a fatwa an individual duty (fard 'ayn) versus a communal one (fard kifaya)? The default is communal, but three exceptional situations make it personally obligatory. Understanding these criteria is essential for a mufti to fulfill his responsibility without falling into concealment of knowledge or disobedience to authority. We will explore each situation and its Quranic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the default ruling
Identify that ifta is normally fard kifaya: if some qualified muftis fulfill it, others are absolved. This means the obligation is on the community, not every individual.
This default applies when there are multiple qualified persons.
2
Recognize the three exceptions
List the three situations where ifta becomes fard 'ayn: (1) the mufti is the only qualified person in the area, (2) the questioner is in urgent need and cannot ask another, (3) the mufti is officially appointed by the ruler.
Each exception has a specific condition; they are not exhaustive.
3
Connect each exception to its dalil
For each exception, state the Quranic verse cited: for (1) and (2) Surah al-Baqarah 2:159 (prohibition of concealing knowledge), for (3) Surah an-Nisa 4:59 (obey those in authority).
The verses provide the legal basis (dalil naqli).
4
Apply the distinctions to a scenario
Given a practical scenario, decide whether ifta is fard kifaya or fard 'ayn. Check the conditions: are there other qualified muftis? Is there urgency? Is the mufti appointed?
Practice ensures mastery of the criteria.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحكام الإفتاء ومنهجه
أصول الإفتاء
٢٨٥
أحكام الإفتاء ومنهجه
وبعد تمهيد بعض هذه القواعد نُريد أن نبحث عن أحكام الفتوى بمعنى أنه متى يجب على المفتى أن يُفْتِى ومتى يحرم عليه ذلك، ومتى يحق له أن يمتنع عن الجواب. ثمّ نذكر إن شاء الله تعالى المنهج الذي يجب أن يختاره المفتى عندما يُستفتى عن حكم شرعي.
متى يجب الإفتاء؟
الأصل فى الإفتاء أنه فرض كفايةٍ على مفت مؤهل إذا وجد عدد من المؤهلين فإن قام به بعضهم سقط عن الباقين. ويكون فرض عين في
الأحوال الآتية:
الأول: إذا استفتى فى مكان لا يوجد فيه مؤهل غيره، وهو يعرف الحكم، لقول الله سبحانه وتعالى: ﴿إِنَّ الَّذِينَ يَكْتُمُونَ مَا أَنزَلْنَا مِنَ الْبَيِّنَاتِ وَالْهُدَىٰ مِن بَعْدِ مَا بَيَّنَّاهُ لِلنَّاسِ فِي الْكِتَابِ ۙ أُولَٰئِكَ يَلْعَنُهُمُ اللَّهُ وَيَلْعَنُهُمُ اللَّاعِنُونَ﴾ [البقرة : ١٥٩]
الثاني: إذا استفتى والمستفتى فى حاجةٍ عاجلةٍ يُخاف عند عدم الإفتاء أن يقع في محظور، كمن استفتى فى حكم من أحكام الصلوة والوقت ضيق لا يمكن للمستفتى أن يسأل فيه غيره، والمفتى يعرف الحكم. وذلك لما تلونا من الآية الكريمة.
الثالث: إذا نصب المرأ المؤهل مُفتياً من قبل ولي الأمر، فيجب عليه عيناً أن يقوم بالإفتاء، وذلك لقول الله سبحانه وتعالى: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا أَطِيعُوا اللَّهَ وَأَطِيعُوا الرَّسُولَ وَأُولِي الْأَمْرِ مِنكُمْ﴾ [النساء : ٥٩]
Rulings of Ifta and Its Methodology
Usul al-Ifta
285
Rulings of Ifta and Its Methodology
After introducing some of these rules, we wish to discuss the rulings of ifta (fatwa): namely, when it is obligatory for the mufti to issue a fatwa, when it is prohibited for him to do so, and when he has the right to refrain from answering. Then we will mention, Allah willing, the methodology that the mufti must adopt when he is asked about a legal ruling.
When is Ifta Obligatory?
The default ruling regarding ifta is that it is a communal obligation (fard kifaya) upon a qualified mufti. If there is a number of qualified individuals, and some of them undertake it, it is lifted from the rest. It becomes an individual obligation (fard 'ayn) in the following situations:
First: If he is asked for a fatwa in a place where there is no other qualified person besides him, and he knows the ruling, due to Allah's statement: 'Indeed, those who conceal what We sent down of clear proofs and guidance after We made it clear for the people in the Scripture – those are cursed by Allah and cursed by those who curse.' [Quran 2:159]
Second: If he is asked for a fatwa and the questioner is in an urgent need, and it is feared that without the fatwa he will fall into a prohibited matter—such as one who asks about a ruling of prayer while the time is so short that the questioner cannot ask anyone else, and the mufti knows the ruling. This is based on the noble verse we just recited.
Third: If a qualified person is appointed as a mufti by the ruler (wali al-amr), then it becomes an individual obligation upon him to issue fatwas, due to Allah's statement: 'O you who have believed, obey Allah and obey the Messenger and those in authority among you.' [Quran 4:59]
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a ruling (hukm) from Usul al-Ifta explaining the obligation of issuing fatwas. The author, Mufti Muhammad Taqi Usmani, begins by stating the default legal status of ifta: it is fard kifaya (communal obligation). This means that if there are multiple qualified muftis, it is sufficient for some to fulfill this duty, and the rest are absolved. He then lists three exceptional situations in which ifta becomes fard 'ayn (individual obligation) for a particular mufti. The first situation is when the mufti is the only qualified person available in a given location; concealing knowledge in such circumstances incurs the curse mentioned in Surah al-Baqarah 2:159. The second situation is when the questioner is in urgent need and cannot consult anyone else; again, withholding knowledge would lead to harm and is prohibited. The third situation is when the mufti has been officially appointed by the ruling authority (uli al-amr); he must then fulfill his duty as part of obedience to those in authority (Quran 4:59). These rules derive directly from Quranic verses, making them dalil naqli evidence. The passage sets the foundational framework for a mufti's responsibilities: generally communal, but individual under specific conditions.

Key Terms
فرض كفاية (fard kifaya): A collective obligation; if some qualified members perform it, the rest are relieved of sin.
فرض عين (fard 'ayn): An individual obligation incumbent upon each qualified person; neglecting it is sinful.
مفتى (mufti): A qualified jurist who issues fatwas (legal rulings).
مستفتى (mustafti): The person seeking a fatwa (the questioner).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the following situations does ifta become fard 'ayn (individual obligation) upon a mufti?
A) When the mufti is the only qualified person in town and knows the ruling.
B) When the mufti is busy with his own studies.
C) When the questioner is wealthy and can pay for the fatwa.
D) When the mufti is tired and wants to rest.

Key Takeaway
Ifta is a communal duty by default; a mufti must issue a fatwa individually only when he is the sole qualified person, when delay causes harm, or when officially appointed.

The Mufti's Duty to Say 'I Don't Know'
Narrations emphasizing a mufti's obligation to admit ignorance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the adab of a mufti to say 'I don't know' and identify at least three proofs from the narrations of the Salaf.

Core Idea
Saying 'I don't know' is a duty for a mufti, not a weakness.

The ability to admit ignorance is a hallmark of true scholarship. Early Muslim leaders like Abu Bakr, Ali, and Ibn Umar modelled this principle. In this lesson, we examine their statements and actions that establish 'I don't know' as a core mufti adab.

Guided Path
1
Recognise the Principle
The universal rule: a mufti must never speak without knowledge. Saying 'I don't know' is an act of taqwa.
This applies regardless of the scholar's status.
2
Study Each Narration
For each narration, identify the narrator, the context, and the exact wording used to admit ignorance.
Note the variety: 'الله أعلم', 'لا أدرى', and the vivid expression of Abu Bakr.
3
Analyse the Chain of Transmission
One chain connects Ahmad, al-Shafi'i, and Malik – a rare and precious isnad. Understand why it is highlighted.
The chain reinforces the weight of the principle.
4
Extract the Adab
From each narration, derive practical lessons for a mufti: humility, fear of Allah, and redirection to other scholars when needed.
Ibn Umar even kissed the Bedouin's hand for accepting his answer.
5
Apply to Modern Fatwa Practice
A mufti today must be willing to say 'I don't know' and research before answering, even if the questioner pressures him.
This protects both the mufti and the questioner from error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحكام الإفتاء ومنهجه
وقد روى عن عائشة رضي الله عنها أنه لما نزل عذرها قبل أبو بكر رضي الله عنها رأسها قالت: قلت: ألأعذَر تَني عند النّبي صلى الله عليه وسلم! فقال أبو بكر: "أيُّ سماءٍ تُظِلَّني وأيُّ أرض تُقِلّني إذا قلتُ ما لا أعلم؟" وعن عُروة التميمي، قال: قال علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه: "وَابَردَها على الكبد!" ثلاث مرات. قالوا: يا أمير المؤمنين، وما ذاك؟ قال: أن يُسأل الرجل عمّا لا يعلم، فيقول: الله أعلم. وروى عن خالد بن أسلم، وهو أخو زيد بن أسلم قال: خرجنا مع عبد الله ابن عمر نَمْشِي فلحقنا أعرابي، فقال: أنت عبد الله بن عمر؟ قال: نعم. فقال: سألت عنك فدللت عليك، فأخبرني أتَرثُ العمة؟ فقال ابنُ عمر: لا أدرى فقال: أنت لا تدرى ولا ندرى؟ قال: نعم، اذهب إلى العلماء بالمدينة فاسألهم فلما أدبر قبل ابن عمر يديه، فقال: نعما قال أبو عبد الرحمن، سئل عما لا يدرى، فقال: لا أدرى. وقد أخرج ابن عبد البر رحمه الله تعالى عن أبي الحسن على بن الحسن، قال: حدثنا صالح بن أحمد بن حنبل قال: حدثني أبي، قال: حدثني محمد بن إدريس الشافعى رحمه الله تعالى قال: سمعت مالك بن أنس يقول: سمعت ابن عجلان يقول: وإذا غَفَل العالم "لا أدرى" أصيبت مقاتله. وهذا من أعزّ الأسانيد من حيثُ يرويه ثلاثة من الأئمة بعضهم من بعض: الإمام أحمد عن الإمام الشافعي، وهو عن الإمام مالك رحمهم الله تعالى. وكذلك روى ابن عبد البر رحمه الله تعالى بسنده إلى حقبة بن مسلم قال: صحبت ابن عمر أربعة وثلاثين شهراً، فكثيراً ما كان يُسئل فيقول: لا أدرى، ثم يلتفت إلى فيقول: أتدرى ما يُريد هؤلاء؟ يُريدون أن يجعلوا ظهورنا جسراً لهم إلى جهنّم. وعن الأثرم تلميذ الإمام أحمد بن حنبل قال: سمعت أحمد بن حنبل رحمه الله تعالى يُكثر أن يقول: لا أدرى. وعن الهيثم بن جميل قال: "شهدت مالكإ سُئل عن ثمان وأربعين مسئلة"
Rulings of Fatwa and its Methodology: It is narrated from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) that when her innocence was revealed (in the slander incident), Abu Bakr (may Allah be pleased with him) kissed her head. She said: I said, 'Do you apologize to me before the Prophet (peace be upon him)?' Abu Bakr said: 'Which heaven would shade me and which earth would carry me if I said what I do not know?' And from Urwa al-Tamimi, he said: Ali ibn Abi Talib (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'How cool it is on the liver!' three times. They said: O Commander of the Faithful, what is that? He said: That a man is asked about what he does not know, and he says: Allah knows best. And it is narrated from Khalid ibn Aslam, the brother of Zayd ibn Aslam, he said: We went out with Abdullah ibn Umar walking, and a Bedouin caught up with us. He said: Are you Abdullah ibn Umar? He said: Yes. He said: I asked about you and was directed to you. So tell me, does the paternal aunt inherit? Ibn Umar said: I don't know. He said: You don't know, and we don't know? He said: Yes, go to the scholars in Madinah and ask them. When he turned away, Ibn Umar kissed his hand and said: How excellent is what Abu Abd al-Rahman said: He was asked about what he did not know, and he said: I don't know. And Ibn Abd al-Barr (may Allah have mercy on him) narrated from Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn al-Hasan, he said: Salih ibn Ahmad ibn Hanbal told us, he said: My father told me, he said: Muhammad ibn Idris al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) told me, he said: I heard Malik ibn Anas say: I heard Ibn Ajlan say: When the scholar neglects 'I don't know', his vital parts are struck. And this is one of the most precious chains because it is narrated by three imams from one another: Imam Ahmad from Imam al-Shafi'i, and he from Imam Malik (may Allah have mercy on them all). And similarly, Ibn Abd al-Barr narrated with his chain to Haqba ibn Muslim, he said: I accompanied Ibn Umar for thirty-four months, and often he would be asked and say: I don't know, then he would turn to me and say: Do you know what these people want? They want to make our backs a bridge for them to Hell. And from al-Athram, a student of Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, he said: I heard Ahmad ibn Hanbal (may Allah have mercy on him) frequently saying: I don't know. And from al-Haytham ibn Jamil, he said: I witnessed Malik being asked about forty-eight issues.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a collection of proofs (dalil) from the early generations establishing a fundamental principle of mufti adab: a scholar must not hesitate to say 'I don't know' when asked about something he does not know. The author, Taqi al-Uthmani, presents these narrations to instill in the mufti the courage to acknowledge ignorance rather than guess or speak without knowledge. The first narration from Aisha and Abu Bakr highlights that speaking without knowledge is a grave sin that even the heavens and earth cannot bear. The story of Ali ibn Abi Talib emphasizes the relief ('coolness on the liver') that comes from deferring to Allah's knowledge. Ibn Umar's interaction with a Bedouin shows his humility despite his high status; he even kisses the Bedouin's hand for accepting his answer. The chain from Ahmad to al-Shafi'i to Malik is highlighted as particularly precious because it links three great imams. The final quote from al-Haytham ibn Jamil shows that Imam Malik was asked numerous questions and presumably answered with 'la adri' when appropriate. This lesson teaches that saying 'I don't know' is not a sign of weakness but of piety and responsibility.

Key Terms
لا أدرى (lā adrī): A statement meaning 'I do not know' that a mufti should utter when he lacks knowledge on a legal issue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the narration in the lesson, what did Abdullah ibn Umar say when asked about the inheritance of a paternal aunt?
A) She inherits a fixed share.
B) She does not inherit.
C) I don't know.
D) Go ask someone else.

Key Takeaway
The mufti must always say 'I don't know' if he does not have certain knowledge, following the example of the Salaf.

Prohibited States for Giving Fatwa: Desire and Anger
Listing two prohibited states for fatwa with textual proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two prohibited states for fatwa (desire and anger) and apply the ruling that a mufti must be calm and impartial.

Core Idea
A mufti must be free from desire and anger—both invalidate the fatwa's integrity.

This lesson covers two critical adab al-mufti: refraining from fatwa based on personal desire or anger. The author cites Quranic and hadith evidence to establish that these states prevent sound reflection and thus make fatwa impermissible. Understanding these prohibitions ensures the mufti upholds the trust of ifta.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first prohibited state
What is the first prohibited state? How does the author define it?
The prohibition is specifically when the mufti leans toward the mustafti out of desire.
2
Identify the second prohibited state
What is the second state? What evidence is provided?
Anger is cited as a primary example of a state preventing proper reflection.
3
Analyze the dalil
For each prohibition, identify the textual evidence (Quran/hadith) and explain how it is applied to the mufti.
The Quran verse warns against following desire; the hadith forbids judging while angry, and the mufti is analogous to a judge.
4
Note the consensus
The text mentions that fatwa by desire is forbidden by consensus. What does this imply?
Consensus (ijma) gives the ruling a higher level of certainty.
5
Apply the ruling
How would you advise a mufti who is angry? What should he do before giving fatwa?
He should wait until he is calm and composed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: إذا كان الإفتاء بهوى وميل مع المستفتى، بحيث يغلبُ الظَّنّ أنّه يتهاون ويداهن معه. قال الله سبحانه وتعالى: ﴿يَدَاوُردُ إِنَّا جَعَلْنَاكَ خَلِيفَةً فِي الْأَرْضِ فَاحْكُمْ بَيْنَ النَّاسِ بِالْحَقِّ وَلَا تَتَّبِعِ الْهَوَى فَيُضِلُّكَ عَن سَبِيلِ اللَّهِ ۚ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ يَضِلُّونَ عَن سَبِيلِ اللَّهِ لَهُمْ عَذَابٌ شَدِيدٌ بِمَا نَسُوا يَوْمَ الْحِسَابِ ﴾ [ ص : ٢٦] وجاء في الإقناع فى مذهب الإمام أحمد : ويحرم الحكم والفتيا بالهوى إجماعاً، وليحذر المفتى أن يميل فى فتياه مع المستفتى، أو مع خصمه. الثَّالث: إذا كان المفتى فى حالةٍ تمنعه من أداء واجبه في الفتوى من التأمل والنظر الصحيح. والدليل على ذلك حديث أبي بكرة رضى الله تعالى عنه قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلّم: 'لايَقْضِيَنَّ حَكَمْ بَيْنَ اثْنَيْنِ وَهُوَ غَضْبَانُ.'
Second: If the fatwa is given with desire and inclination towards the mustafti (the one seeking fatwa), such that it is highly probable that he is lenient and flatters him. Allah Most High says: 'O David, indeed We have made you a vicegerent upon the earth, so judge between the people with truth and do not follow desire, lest it lead you astray from the way of Allah. Indeed, those who go astray from the way of Allah will have a severe punishment because they forgot the Day of Reckoning.' [Sad: 26] And it is stated in al-Iqna' in the Hanbali school: 'Judgment and fatwa according to desire are forbidden by consensus. The mufti must beware of inclining in his fatwa with the mustafti or with his adversary.' Third: If the mufti is in a state that prevents him from performing his duty in fatwa, i.e., from reflection and proper consideration. The evidence for that is the hadith of Abu Bakra (may Allah be pleased with him) who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'A judge should not judge between two persons while he is angry.'
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage enumerates two more prohibitions for the mufti. The first prohibition (الثاني) is giving fatwa based on desire (hawā) or favoritism toward the questioner. The author cites Quran 38:26, warning against following desire. He then quotes from the Hanbali text al-Iqna' that such fatwa is forbidden by consensus. The second prohibition (الثالث) is giving fatwa while in a state that impedes proper reasoning, such as anger. The proof is the well-known hadith that a judge should not judge while angry, by analogy extended to the mufti. The lesson reinforces that fatwa requires sound judgment free from emotional or personal bias.

Key Terms
إفتاء بهوى (iftā' bi-hawā): Giving a fatwa based on personal desire or inclination toward the questioner, which is prohibited by consensus.
حال غضب (hāl ghaḍab): A state of anger that prevents the mufti from proper reflection and fair judgment, making fatwa impermissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is permissible as long as the fatwa is correct.
B) It is disliked but not forbidden.

D) It is recommended to wait until he calms down.

Key Takeaway
A mufti must be free from desire and anger when giving fatwa; both are prohibited by Quran and Sunnah and render the fatwa invalid.

When a Mufti Must Not Give Fatwa: Prohibited States
Lists states prohibiting a mufti from issuing fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the states that prohibit a mufti from issuing a fatwa and apply the rule using the hadith and qiyas.

Core Idea
A mufti must not issue a fatwa when biased or emotionally disturbed — only calm deliberation ensures justice.

This passage from Usul al-Ifta explains two critical prohibitions: fatwa based on personal desire, and fatwa while in a disturbed state. It uses Quran, hadith, and consensus to establish that the mufti must be in full control of his faculties. Understanding these prohibitions is essential for preserving the integrity of fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of prohibition
The passage divides prohibited states into two main categories: (1) issuing fatwa out of personal desire or bias (hawa), and (2) issuing fatwa while in any state that impairs deliberation (anger, fear, hunger, etc.).
The first is based on Quran and consensus; the second on hadith and qiyas.
2
Locate the evidence for each category
For desire, the Quranic verse to Prophet Dawud (Surah Sad, 26) is cited. For impaired deliberation, the hadith of Abu Bakra ('A judge should not judge while angry') is cited and extended to the mufti by qiyas.
Also note the consensus from al-Iqna': 'It is forbidden to judge and give fatwa according to desire by consensus.'
3
List the specific states that impair deliberation
The passage explicitly mentions: anger, fear, lust, intense sadness, intense joy, drowsiness, hunger, severe illness, extreme heat or cold, and the urge to relieve oneself (mudafa'at al-akhbatayn). These all take the mufti away from moderation.
These are not exhaustive but representative of states that disturb concentration.
4
Determine the ruling and its strength
When emotion overcomes correct thinking, it is obligatory (wajib) for the mufti to refrain from issuing fatwa until he returns to his normal state. The prohibition is derived from definitive evidence (Quran, hadith, ijma).
The ruling is clear: it is not permissible to give fatwa in any of these states.
5
Apply the rule through analogy (qiyas)
The hadith explicitly mentions a judge, but the passage applies it to a mufti by qiyas because both roles require sound judgment. Similarly, any state that would prevent a judge from judging prevents a mufti from giving fatwa.
This qiyas is strong because the illa (effective cause) is common: the need for correct deliberation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: إذا كان الإفتاء بهوى وميل مع المستفتى، بحيث يغلبُ الظَّنّ أنّه يتهاون ويداهن معه. قال الله سبحانه وتعالى: ﴿يَدَاوُردُ إِنَّا جَعَلْنَاكَ خَلِيفَةً فِي الْأَرْضِ فَاحْكُمْ بَيْنَ النَّاسِ بِالْحَقِّ وَلَا تَتَّبِعِ الْهَوَى فَيُضِلُّكَ عَن سَبِيلِ اللَّهِ ۚ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ يَضِلُّونَ عَن سَبِيلِ اللَّهِ لَهُمْ عَذَابٌ شَدِيدٌ بِمَا نَسُوا يَوْمَ الْحِسَابِ ﴾ [ ص : ٢٦] وجاء في الإقناع فى مذهب الإمام أحمد : ويحرم الحكم والفتيا بالهوى إجماعاً، وليحذر المفتى أن يميل فى فتياه مع المستفتى، أو مع خصمه. (۲) الثَّالث: إذا كان المفتى فى حالةٍ تمنعه من أداء واجبه في الفتوى من التأمل والنظر الصحيح. والدليل على ذلك حديث أبي بكرة رضى الله تعالى عنه قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلّم: "لايَقْضِيَنَّ حَكَمْ بَيْنَ اثْنَيْنِ وَهُوَ غَضْبَانُ. " ولذلك قالوا: ومما ينبغي للمفتى مراعاته أن لا يُفتي حال اشتغال قلبه بغضب أو رهبة أو شهوة ممّا يُخرجه عن الاعتدال. وكذلك شدة الحُزن وشدة الفرح ونحوه. فإن غلب انفعاله علي صحة تفكيره، وجب عليه أن يكُفَّ عن الإفتاء إلى أن يعود إلى طبيعته، وكذلك إن كان به نعاس أو جوع أو مرض شديد أو حرٌ مُزْعِج أو بَردَ مُؤلم، أو مُدافعة الأخبثين.
Second: If the fatwa is given with desire and inclination towards the one seeking the fatwa, such that it is likely that he is being lenient and flattering him. Allah Almighty said: 'O David, indeed We have made you a vicegerent upon the earth, so judge between the people with truth and do not follow desire, for it will lead you astray from the way of Allah. Indeed, those who go astray from the way of Allah will have a severe punishment for having forgotten the Day of Reckoning.' [Sad: 26]. And it came in al-Iqna' according to the school of Imam Ahmad: It is forbidden to judge and give fatwa according to desire by consensus. And let the mufti beware of inclining in his fatwa towards the one seeking the fatwa or towards his opponent. Third: If the mufti is in a state that prevents him from fulfilling his duty in the fatwa of deliberation and correct consideration. The evidence for that is the hadith of Abu Bakra, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: The Messenger of Allah, peace and blessings be upon him, said: 'A judge should not judge between two people while he is angry.' Therefore, they said: Among the things that the mufti should observe is that he should not give a fatwa while his heart is occupied with anger, fear, or desire, which takes him away from moderation. Likewise, intense sadness, intense joy, and the like. If his emotion overcomes the correctness of his thinking, it is obligatory for him to refrain from giving fatwa until he returns to his normal state. Likewise, if he has drowsiness, hunger, severe illness, disturbing heat, painful cold, or the urge to relieve one of the two impurities.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage addresses two categories of prohibited states for a mufti. First, issuing a fatwa out of personal desire or bias toward the questioner is strictly forbidden, supported by the Quranic verse rebuking David (AS) and by consensus across schools. Second, any state that impairs the mufti's ability to deliberate properly—anger, fear, lust, extreme emotion, drowsiness, hunger, illness, temperature extremes, or bodily urges—makes fatwa-giving impermissible. The hadith about not judging while angry is cited as an analogy (qiyas) for muftis. The ruling is definitive: the mufti must wait until his mental clarity returns.

Key Terms
الهوى (al-hawa): Personal desire or inclination, especially a bias toward the questioner that compromises objectivity.
مدافعة الأخبثين (mudafa'at al-akhbatayn): The urge to urinate or defecate, which disturbs concentration.
إجماع (ijma'): Consensus of scholars, here cited as supporting the prohibition of fatwa by desire.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When he is feeling sad.
B) When he is in a state of drowsiness.
C) When emotion overcomes his correct thinking.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
A mufti must refrain from fatwa when his objectivity is compromised by bias or any state that impairs sound reasoning.

When to Withhold Fatwa: Refraining for the Sake of Preventing Harm
Mufti adab: situations to refrain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two situations in which a mufti should refrain from giving a fatwa and apply the principle of dar' al-mafasid (preventing harm) to decide when to withhold.

Core Idea
A mufti is not obliged to answer every question; he may refrain when harm is feared or when the question is useless.

This lesson explores the mufti's discretion to withhold fatwa. While a mufti is a source of guidance, he is not a machine that outputs rulings on demand. The passage teaches that preventing harm (dar' al-mafasid) takes precedence over providing an answer, and that trivial questions can be set aside. This is a crucial aspect of mufti adab, safeguarding the community from misuse of rulings.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the general principle
The passage opens by stating that a mufti is not obliged to answer every question. He should answer only when he sees benefit and is safe from fitna.
This is a discretionary right, not a license to be rude.
2
Identify the first situation: fear of fitna
If the mufti fears that his answer will be used to cause discord, harm, or be misapplied, he should refrain. This is based on the maxim 'dar' al-mafasid muqaddam 'ala jalb al-masalih' (preventing harm takes precedence over bringing benefit).
Al-Ajuri's quote emphasizes politeness in declining.
3
Identify the second situation: trivial or useless questions
If the question is of no practical benefit or concerns something that should not be delved into (like unnecessary hypotheticals), the mufti may decline.
The example of the Companions shows they asked only thirteen issues, implying restraint.
4
Apply the adab of declining
When declining, the mufti should do so gently and redirect the questioner to what is better for him, as per al-Ajuri's advice.
This preserves respect and encourages good conduct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الامتناع عن الفتوى
وليس من واجب المفتى أن يُجيب عن كل سؤالٍ يُطرح أمامه فى كل حال. وإنّما يُجيب حيث يرى مصلحةً في الجواب، ويأمن الفتنة، فينبغي أن يمتنع عن الإفتاء في الأحوال الآتية:
الأول: إذا خَشِى المُفتى أنّ المستفتى يُثير فتنةً بعد الحصول على الجواب، أو أن الإفتاء فى المسئلة تُؤدى إلى مفسدة ظاهرة، أو أنه يصرف الفتوى إلى غير وجهها، لأن دفع المفسدة مقدم على جلب المصلحة. قال الآجري رحمه الله تعالى: "وإذا سئل عن مسئلةٍ فعَلم أنها من مسائل الشَّغَبُ ، وممّا يُورِثُ بين المسلمين الفتنة استعفى منها، وردّ السائل إلى ماهو أولى به على أرفق ما يكون "
الثاني: إذاكان السؤال ممّا ينبغي عدم الخوض فيه لكونه مما لا يعنى، وليس هناك نفع عملي في معرفة الجواب عنه. وقد روى عن ابن عبّاس رضى الله تعالى عنهما قال: "ما رأيت قوماً كانوا خيراً من أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلّم، ما سألوه إلا عن ثلاث عشرة مسألة حتى قبض، كلهن في القرآن...
Refraining from giving fatwa. It is not obligatory for the mufti to answer every question put before him in every situation. Rather, he gives an answer where he sees benefit in answering and is safe from fitna. So he should refrain from giving fatwa in the following situations: First: If the mufti fears that the questioner will stir up fitna after obtaining the answer, or that giving a fatwa on the issue leads to manifest harm, or that he will misuse the fatwa, because preventing harm takes precedence over bringing benefit. Al-Ajuri, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'If he is asked about an issue and he knows that it is among the issues of disturbance and that it causes fitna among the Muslims, he should excuse himself from it and direct the questioner to what is better for him in the gentlest way possible.' Second: If the question is something that should not be delved into because it is of no concern and there is no practical benefit in knowing the answer. It is narrated from Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, that he said: 'I have not seen a people better than the companions of the Messenger of Allah, peace and blessings be upon him. They asked him only about thirteen issues until he died, all of which are in the Quran...'
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage establishes that a mufti is not obliged to answer every question. He may withhold fatwa in two circumstances: (1) when he fears the answer will cause fitna (discord) or be misused, because preventing harm outweighs potential benefit; (2) when the question is trivial or practically useless. Al-Ajuri's quote emphasizes politeness in declining. The example of the Companions shows they asked only relevant questions. This is a principle of mufti adab: discretion and guarding against abuse of religious rulings.

Key Terms
الفتنة (al-fitna): Discord, trial, or conflict that may result from a fatwa being misused or misunderstood.
الشَّغَبُ (al-shaghab): Disturbance, trouble, or contentious issues that lead to conflict.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which situation should a mufti refrain from giving a fatwa?
A) When he does not know the answer.
B) When he fears the answer will cause fitna.
C) When the questioner is a non-Muslim.
D) When the question is about a minor issue.

Key Takeaway
A mufti may withhold a fatwa when it will cause harm or when the question is pointless; preventing harm takes precedence.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti

When a Mufti Should Refuse to Answer
Norms discouraging mufti from answering irrelevant/speculative questions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which types of questions a Hanafi mufti should refuse to answer, explain the rationale (dalil) and apply the principle of avoiding unnecessary speculation in fatwa.

Core Idea
A mufti must refuse questions that are rare, irrelevant, or beyond the questioner's comprehension.

This lesson examines the adab (norms) for a mufti regarding when to decline answering a question. Drawing on hadith, the practice of the Salaf, and Hanafi juristic guidance, the passage classifies three categories of questions to avoid. Understanding this helps the mufti preserve the integrity of fatwa and direct the questioner to what is beneficial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of questions to avoid
Read the passage and list the three categories mentioned by Ibn 'Abidin and others.
The categories are: 1) rare/difficult matters seldom encountered, 2) theological/historical details not obligatory, 3) questions beyond the questioner's intellect.
2
Understand the hadith 'Halaka al-mutanatti'un'
Analyze how the hadith is interpreted to discourage extreme questioning.
The hadith warns against tanattu' (going to extremes), which includes asking difficult, rare questions.
3
Analyze the examples from Imam Ahmad
Examine the story about wudu with lime water and the questioner who neglected essential supplications.
Imam Ahmad criticized the questioner for asking about a rare matter while being ignorant of daily necessities.
4
Apply the principle to determine when a mufti should refuse
Use the three categories as a checklist when evaluating questions.
If a question fits any category, the mufti should not answer and should guide the questioner to practical matters.
5
Recognize the underlying legal maxim
Connect this norm to broader Hanafi principles like 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are judged by their objectives).
The goal of fatwa is guidance, not speculation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ورد في الحديث المرفوع: "هَلَكَ المُتَنَطِّعُونَ" وقد فسّره بعض العلماء بالغالين في الخوض فيما لا يعنيهم، والمتعنتين في السؤال عن عويص المسائل الذي يندر وقوعها. وكان السلف يكرهون أن يُكثر عامة الناس الأسئلة في الأمور التي هم في غنى عنها في حياتهم العمليّة، فينبغى أن لا يُشَجّعهم المفتى على مثل هذه الأسئلة، وأن يَهْدِيَهم إلى ما يَعْنِيهم. وقال أحمد بن حبّان القطيعي: "دخلت على أبي عبدالله ( يعنى الإمام أحمد رحمه الله تعالى فقلت: أتوضأ بماء النورة؟ قال: لا أحبّ ذلك. فقلت: أتوضأ بماء الباقلاء؟ قال: ما أحب ذلك. قال: ثم قمت، فتعلّق بثوبي، وقال: أيش تقول إذا دخلت المسجد؟ فسكت فقال: أيش تقول إذا خرجت من المسجد؟ فسكت، فقال: اذهب فتعلم هذا." وأراد الإمام رحمه الله تعالى أن الوضوء بماء النورة وماء الباقلاء أمر نادر ربما لا يحتاج إليه السائل، فعاب عليه أن يخوض فيه مع جهله ما يحتاج إليه خمس مرات كل يوم، وهو الدُّعاء المأثور عند دخول المسجد وعند الخروج منه. وسئل الإمام أحمد رحمه الله تعالى مرةً عن يأجوج ومأجوج: أمسلمون هم ؟ فقال للسائل: "أحكمت العلم حتى تسأل عن ذا؟" وسئل عن مسألة فى اللعان، فقال: "سَلْ رَحِمَك الله عمّا ابتليت به." وقال ابن عابدين رحمه الله تعالى: "يُكره الجدل في أن لقمان وذا القرنين وذا الكفل أنبياء أم لا؟ وينبغي أن لا يسأل الإنسانُ عَمَّا لا حاجة إليه، كأن يقول: كيف هبط جبريل؟ وعلى أي صورة رآه النبي صلى الله عليه وسلّم؟ وحين رآه على صورة البشر ، هل بقي ملكاً أم لا؟ وأين الجنّة والنار؟ ومتى. الساعة ونُزول عيسى؟ وإسماعيل أفضل أم إسحاق؟ وأيهما الذبيح؟ وفاطمة أفضل من عائشة أم لا؟ وأبوا النبي كانا على أي دين؟ وما دين أبي طالب؟ ومن المهدي؟ إلى غير ذلك ممّا لا تجب معرفته ولم يَردِ التّكليف به. الثالث: إذا كانت المسئلة مما لا يَسَعُه عقل السائل وفهمه، ولا تتعلق بها حاجة عملية له، مثل المتشابهات أو دقائق علم الكلام، أو المسائل التي لا يمكن أن يُقطع فيها، وقال سيدنا علي بن أبى طالب رضى الله تعالى عنه: " حدثوا الناس بما يعرفون أتُحبّون أن يُكتب الله ورسوله؟ )" قال الإمام القرافي رحمه الله تعالى: "وينبغى للمفتى إذا جاءته فتيا في شأن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلّم أو فيما يتعلّق بالربوبية، يُسأل فيها عن أمور لا تصلح لذلك السائل، لكونه من العوام الجلف، أو يُسأل عن المُعْضلات ودقائق أصول الديانات ومتشابه الآيات والأمور التي لا يخوض فيها إلا كبار العلماء، فلا يُجيبه أصلاً، ويُظهر له الإنكار على مثل هذا، ويقول له: اشتغل بمَا يَعْنيك من السؤال عن صلاتك وأمورِ مُعاملاتك.
And it is reported in the marfu' hadith: 'The mutanatti'un (those who go to extremes) are destroyed.' And some scholars have interpreted it as those who delve deeply into what does not concern them, and those who ask difficult questions about rare occurrences. The Salaf disliked that the common people ask many questions about matters they are free from in their practical lives. So the mufti should not encourage them in such questions, and should guide them to what concerns them. Ahmad ibn Hibban al-Qati'i said: 'I entered upon Abu Abdullah (meaning Imam Ahmad, may Allah have mercy on him) and said: Should I perform wudu with lime water? He said: I do not like that. I said: Should I perform wudu with bean water? He said: I do not like that. Then I stood up, and he grabbed my garment and said: What do you say when you enter the mosque? I was silent. He said: What do you say when you leave the mosque? I was silent. He said: Go and learn this.' And the Imam (may Allah have mercy on him) meant that wudu with lime water and bean water is a rare matter that the questioner probably does not need, so he criticized him for delving into it while being ignorant of what he needs five times every day, which is the supplication reported upon entering and leaving the mosque. And Imam Ahmad (may Allah have mercy on him) was once asked about Gog and Magog: Are they Muslims? He said to the questioner: 'Have you mastered knowledge that you ask about this?' And he was asked about a matter concerning li'an (mutual imprecation), and he said: 'Ask, may Allah have mercy on you, about what you have been afflicted with.' And Ibn 'Abidin (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'It is disliked to debate whether Luqman, Dhul Qarnayn, and Dhul Kifl are prophets or not. And a person should not ask about what is not needed, such as saying: How did Jibril descend? In what form did the Prophet (ﷺ) see him? And when he saw him in human form, did he remain an angel or not? Where are Paradise and Hell? When is the Hour and the descent of Jesus? Is Isma'il better or Ishaq? Which one was the sacrifice? Is Fatima better than Aisha or not? What religion were the Prophet's parents? What was the religion of Abu Talib? Who is the Mahdi? And other things whose knowledge is not obligatory and regarding which there is no obligation.' The third: If the question is such that the intellect and understanding of the questioner cannot encompass it, and there is no practical need related to it, such as ambiguous matters, subtleties of theology, or matters that cannot be definitively settled. And Sayyiduna 'Ali ibn Abi Talib (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'Speak to people about what they know; do you want Allah and His Messenger to be denied?' Al-Qarafi (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'It is befitting for the mufti, when a fatwa comes to him concerning the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) or regarding divinity, asking about matters not suitable for that questioner because he is a coarse commoner, or he is asked about difficult problems, subtleties of the principles of religions, ambiguous verses, and matters that only senior scholars delve into, then he should not answer him at all, and should show disapproval of such, and say to him: Occupy yourself with what concerns you from asking about your prayer and your transactions.'
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a set of rulings (adab al-mufti) regarding the types of questions a mufti should avoid answering. The author presents three categories. First, questions about rare and difficult matters that seldom occur (like wudu with lime water) and which do not concern the questioner. The mufti should discourage such questions and guide the questioner to practical religious matters. Second, questions about theological or historical details that have no bearing on obligatory knowledge or practical life (like whether Luqman was a prophet). Ibn 'Abidin lists many examples. Third, questions that are beyond the comprehension of the average person (like ambiguous verses or fine points of theology). The mufti should not answer, but show disapproval and direct the questioner to matters of prayer and transactions. The core principle is that the mufti should not facilitate unnecessary or harmful speculation, but rather prioritize beneficial knowledge. The hadith about 'halaka al-mutanatti'un' warns against excessive questioning. The examples from Imam Ahmad and Ali (ra) reinforce this.

Key Terms
المُتَنَطِّعُونَ (al-mutanatti'un): Those who go to extremes, delving into what does not concern them and asking difficult, rare questions.
عويص المسائل (awiys al-masa'il): Difficult and rare issues that cause hardship and are seldom encountered.
المعضلات (al-mu'dilat): Perplexing problems that are hard to solve, often in theology or ambiguous texts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the third category of questions a mufti should avoid answering?
A) Questions about matters that are beneficial for the questioner
B) Questions that are beyond the intellect and comprehension of the questioner
C) Questions about urgent practical matters
D) Questions about things the mufti knows well

Key Takeaway
A mufti should prioritize practical beneficial knowledge and avoid encouraging speculation on rare, irrelevant, or incomprehensible matters.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti
intermediate
6 min
When a Mufti Should Refuse to Answer a Question
Guidelines for muftis on rejecting inappropriate or unnecessary questions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three categories of questions that a mufti should refuse to answer and apply the appropriate response (refusal with redirection) to each.

Core Idea
A mufti must sometimes refuse to answer: from excellence of Islam is leaving what does not concern you.

This lesson examines guidelines from classical Hanafi and Maliki scholars on when a mufti should not answer a question. Not every inquiry deserves a response; some are harmful or irrelevant. The passage from Ibn 'Abidin, al-Qarafi, and anecdotes of early imams provide clear criteria for refusal.

Guided Path
1
Identify categories of inappropriate questions
The passage lists several types: (1) questions about unnecessary theological disputes (e.g., prophethood of Luqman, Dhu'l-Qarnayn, Dhu'l-Kifl); (2) questions about unnecessary details (e.g., form of angels, location of Paradise, relative merits of prophets); (3) questions beyond the questioner's capacity (e.g., ambiguous verses, subtle ahkam, matters with no definitive answer).
These categories overlap; the common thread is lack of practical necessity.
2
Determine the mufti's response
For each category, the mufti should not answer. Instead, he should show disapproval and redirect the questioner to practical matters like prayer and transactions. This is explicitly stated by al-Qarafi.
The response may include scolding in extreme cases, as seen with Imam Ahmad and Sufyan al-Thawri.
3
Analyze historical precedents
The passage includes anecdotes: Imam Ahmad scolded a judge for asking about children of polytheists; Sufyan al-Thawri rebuked a questioner on the same topic; Ziyad al-Qurtubi responded to a king asking about the scales of judgment by writing the hadith about leaving what does not concern him.
These show that even high-status questioners are not exempt.
4
Apply the guiding hadith
The hadith 'Min husni islam al-mar'i tarkuhu ma la ya'nih' (From the excellence of a person's Islam is leaving that which does not concern him) is the core principle. The mufti applies it both to his own conduct and as a response.
The mufti may cite this hadith directly, as did the author's father.
5
Synthesize the mufti's adab
Combine the criteria: the mufti must assess each question, and if it falls into the prohibited categories, he must refuse and redirect. This preserves the dignity of knowledge and protects the questioner from confusion.
The mufti's refusal is an act of 'adab' (propriety), not rudeness.

(1) رد المحتار، قبيل كتاب الفرائض، ٦:٧٥٤ (۲) (۳) ۲۹ علقه البخاري في صحيحه كتاب العلم، باب من خص بالعلم قوما دون قوم كراهية أن لا يفهموا" (۳) الإحكام في تمييز الفتاوى عن الأحكام للقرافي، التنبيه العاشر ص ٢٦٤ وقد سأل ابن الشافعي الذي ولى قضاء حلب الإمام أحمد رحمه الله تعالى عن عاقبة ذرارى المشركين أو المسلمين، فصاح به رحمه الله تعالى، وقال له: "هذه مسائل أهل الزيغ، مالك ولهذه المسائل؟" وكذلك سئل سفيان الثوري رحمه الله تعالى عن أطفال المشركين، فصاح بالسائل وقال له: صبي! أنت تسأل عن ذا؟ () 19 وكان والدى العلامة المفتى محمد شفیع رحمه الله تعالى حينما يُسئل عن مثل هذه الأمور يكتب فى الجواب حديث رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلّم: " مِنْ حُسْنِ إِسْلاَمِ الْمَرْأَ تَرَكُهُ مَالاً يَعْنِيهِ. ) ثم وجدت عن أحد كبار تلامذة الإمام مالك فعل مثل ذلك مع أحد الملوك. وهو زياد بن عبد الرحمن القُرطبي الملقب بشبطون. فقد حكى عنه القاضي عياض رحمه الله تعالى ما یلى قال :حبيب كنا جُلوساً عند زياد، فأتاه كتاب من أحد الملوك، فمده مدةً، أى بل قلمه بلةٌ من الحبر، فكتب فيه، ثمّ طبع الكتاب ونقذبه الرسول. فقال زياد أتدرون عمّا سأل صاحب هذا الكتاب؟ سأل عن كِفَّتى ميزان الأعمال يوم القيامة، أمِنْ ذَهَب هو أم من وَرَق؟ فكتبت إليه: حدثنا مالك، عن ابن شهاب، قال: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلّم: مِنْ حُسْنِ إِسْلاَمِ الْمَرْأَ تَرْكُهُ مَا لاَ يَعْنِيْهِ وَسَتَرِدُ فَتَعْلَمُ. . (1) الآداب الشرعيّة والمنح المرعية ٢:٧٢ (۲) أخرجه الترمذي فى أبواب الزهد، حدیث ۲۳۱۷ عن أبي هريرة رضى الله تعالى عنه، وعن علي بن الحسين مرسلا. (۳) نقله شيخنا عبد الفتاح أبو غدة في حاشيته على إحكام القرافي ص ٢٦٥ عن ترتيب المدارك للقاضي عياض، ترجمة زياد بن عبد الرحمن، ۳:۱۲۰

And a person should not ask about that which he has no need of, such as saying: How did Jibril descend? In what form did the Prophet, peace be upon him, see him? When he saw him in human form, did he remain an angel or not? Where are Paradise and Hell? When is the Hour and the descent of Jesus? Is Isma'il better or Ishaq? Which of them was the intended sacrifice? Is Fatima better than Aisha or not? Upon which religion were the Prophet's parents? What was the religion of Abu Talib? Who is the Mahdi? And other such things that are not obligatory to know and regarding which there is no religious charge. Third: If the matter is such that the questioner's mind and understanding cannot contain it, and it does not pertain to any practical need for him, such as the ambiguous verses, the subtleties of scholastic theology, or matters that cannot be definitively settled. And sayyiduna Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'Speak to people with what they know. Do you wish that Allah and His Messenger be denied?'" (1)11 Imam al-Qarafi, may Allah have mercy on him, said: "It is befitting for the mufti that when a fatwa comes to him concerning the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, or pertaining to Lordship, and he is asked about matters that are not suitable for that questioner because he is of the common rough folk, or he is asked about difficult problems, subtleties of the principles of religions, ambiguous verses, and matters that only senior scholars delve into, he should not answer him at all, and he should show him disapproval for such [questions], and tell him: Occupy yourself with what concerns you, by asking about your prayer and your transactions." (1) Radd al-Muhtar, before the Book of Fara'id, 6:754 (2) (3) 29 Al-Bukhari recorded it in his Sahih, Book of Knowledge, Chapter: Whoever Specified a Group with Knowledge Excluding Others for Fear That They Would Not Understand (3) Al-Ihkam fi Tamyiz al-Fatawa 'an al-Ahkam by al-Qarafi, the Tenth Warning, p. 264. And Ibn al-Shafi'i, who was appointed judge of Aleppo, asked Imam Ahmad, may Allah have mercy on him, about the outcome of the children of polytheists or Muslims. So he shouted at him and said: "These are the questions of the deviant. What do you have to do with these questions?" Similarly, Sufyan al-Thawri, may Allah have mercy on him, was asked about the children of polytheists, so he shouted at the questioner and said: "O lad! You ask about that?" (19) And my father, the scholar and mufti Muhammad Shafi', may Allah have mercy on him, when asked about such matters, would write in the answer the hadith of the Messenger of Allah: "Indeed, from the excellence of a person's Islam is that he leaves alone that which does not concern him." Then I found that one of the senior students of Imam Malik did the same with a king. He is Ziyad ibn Abd al-Rahman al-Qurtubi, nicknamed Shabtun. Qadi 'Iyad, may Allah have mercy on him, narrated the following: He said: Habib said: We were sitting with Ziyad when a letter came to him from one of the kings. He stretched his hand and moistened his pen with a drop of ink, wrote in it, then sealed the letter and dispatched it with the messenger. Ziyad said: Do you know what the author of this letter asked? He asked about the two pans of the scale of deeds on the Day of Judgment: are they of gold or of paper? So I wrote to him: Malik narrated to us, from Ibn Shihab, who said: The Messenger of Allah said: "Indeed, from the excellence of a person's Islam is that he leaves alone that which does not concern him, and you will return and know." (1) Al-Adab al-Shar'iyyah wa al-Minah al-Mardiyyah 2:72 (2) Narrated by al-Tirmidhi in the Chapters on Asceticism, hadith 2317, from Abu Hurayra, and from Ali ibn al-Husayn as a mursal. (3) Reported by our Shaykh Abd al-Fattah Abu Ghuddah in his footnote to Ihkam al-Qarafi, p. 265, from Tartib al-Madarik by Qadi Iyad, biography of Ziyad ibn Abd al-Rahman, 3:120.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage addresses the etiquettes of a mufti in dealing with questions, specifically when it is appropriate to refuse an answer. It begins by quoting Ibn Abidin, who lists categories of questions that are disliked or should not be asked: those that involve unnecessary theological disputation (like the prophethood of certain figures), questions that are not practically beneficial (like the exact form of angels, the location of Paradise and Hell, the timing of eschatological events, relative merits of prophets and companions, religious status of the Prophet's relatives, and identity of the Mahdi), and questions that are beyond the questioner's intellectual capacity (like mutashabihat, subtle theological issues, and matters that cannot be definitively resolved). Ibn Abidin also cites Ali's instruction to speak to people at their level of understanding. Next, al-Qarafi advises the mufti to outright refuse and express disapproval when a layperson asks about matters pertaining to the Prophet's person or Lordship, or complex theological issues, directing them instead to practical concerns like prayer and transactions. The passage then illustrates these principles with historical anecdotes: Imam Ahmad scolded Ibn al-Shafi'i for asking about the fate of children of polytheists, calling it a question of deviants. Sufyan al-Thawri similarly scolded a questioner about the same topic. The author's father, Mufti Muhammad Shafi', would respond to such questions by writing the hadith 'From the excellence of a person's Islam is leaving that which does not concern him.' Finally, the story of Ziyad al-Qurtubi (Shabtun) shows the same approach: when a king asked about the material of the scales on Judgment Day, Ziyad simply wrote the same hadith and avoided answering. The consistent message is that a mufti must guard against wasting time and confusing people with speculative questions, and should instead redirect them to practical religious duties.

Key Terms
المفتى (al-mufti): The person qualified to issue fatwas (Islamic legal opinions). In this text, the mufti is addressed as one who must judge the appropriateness of questions.
الجدل (al-jadal): Disputation or argumentation, especially about matters that are not practically relevant. The text says it is makruh (disliked) in the specified topics.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did Imam Ahmad do when asked about the fate of children of polytheists?
A) He gave a detailed theological answer.
B) He shouted at the questioner and rebuked him.
C) He remained silent and refused to respond.
D) He wrote the hadith about leaving what does not concern one.

Key Takeaway
A mufti must assess each question and refuse to answer—redirecting to practical matters—when the question is unnecessary, beyond the questioner's capacity, or potentially harmful.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti
beginner
6 min
Etiquette of Avoiding Hypothetical and Useless Questions
Stories of scholars rebuking speculative questions about unseen matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify speculative questions (ma la ya'nihi) and apply the etiquette of refraining from answering them in fatwa practice.

Core Idea
A mufti should not answer speculative questions; the hadith commands leaving what does not concern you.

This lesson presents narrations from early imams demonstrating the proper response to hypothetical questions about unseen matters. The hadith 'Min husni islami al-mar'i tarkuhu ma la ya'nihi' serves as a guiding principle. As a mufti, you must distinguish between beneficial education and idle speculation.

Guided Path
1
Classify the question
Determine if the question is about a matter that has occurred (waqi') or is hypothetical/unseen (ma lam yaqa'). If it is speculative about matters beyond human knowledge, it falls under 'ma la ya'nihi'.
Questions about the details of the afterlife, the nature of divine attributes, or events that never happened are typical examples.
2
Invoke the hadith principle
Recall the hadith: 'Part of the perfection of one's Islam is leaving what does not concern him.' This principle discourages engaging in matters that do not affect one's actions or faith.
This hadith is cited by the author as the basis for the prohibition.
3
Consider the questioner's level
Differentiate between a layperson asking out of curiosity and a student of knowledge asking for fiqh training. The Hanafi school permits the latter but discourages the former.
The stories in the passage address laypeople, not scholars in training.
4
Respond appropriately
If the question is speculative and the questioner is a layperson, respond with the hadith or politely decline. Do not engage in detailed speculation.
The author's father, Mufti Muhammad Shafi, would write the hadith as his entire answer.

وقد سأل ابن الشافعي الذى ولى قضاء حلب الإمام أحمد رحمه الله تعالى عن عاقبة ذرارى المشركين أو المسلمين، فصاح به رحمه الله تعالى، وقال له: "هذه مسائل أهل الزيغ، مالك ولهذه المسائل؟" وكذلك سئل سفيان الثوري رحمه الله تعالى عن أطفال المشركين، فصاح بالسائل وقال له: صبي! أنت تسأل عن ذا؟ () وكان والدى العلامة المفتى محمد شفي رحمه الله تعالى حينما يُسئل عن مثل هذه الأمور يكتب فى الجواب حديث رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلّم: "مِنْ حُسْنِ إِسْلاَمِ الْمَرْأَ تَرَكُهُ مَالاً يَعْنِيهِ." ثم وجدت عن أحد كبار تلامذة الإمام مالك فعل مثل ذلك مع أحد الملوك. وهو زياد بن عبد الرحمن القرطبي الملقب بشبطون. فقد حكى عنه القاضي عياض رحمه الله تعالى ما يلى: قال حبيب: كنا جلوساً عند زياد، فأتاه كتاب من أحد الملوك، فمده مدةً، أى بل قلمه بلةٌ من الحبر، فكتب فيه، ثمّ طبع الكتاب ونقذبه الرسول. فقال زياد: أتدرون عمّا سأل صاحب هذا الكتاب؟ سأل عن كِفَّتى ميزان الأعمال يوم القيامة، أمِنْ ذَهَب هو أم من وَرَق؟ فكتبت إليه: حدثنا مالك، عن ابن شهاب، قال: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلّم: مِنْ حُسْنِ إِسْلاَمِ الْمَرْأَ تَرْكُهُ مَا لاَ يَعْنِيْهِ وَسَتَرِدُ فَتَعْلَمُ.
And Ibn al-Shafi'i, who was appointed judge of Aleppo, asked Imam Ahmad (may Allah have mercy on him) about the final outcome of the offspring of polytheists or Muslims, so he shouted at him and said: 'These are questions of the people of deviation; what business do you have with such questions?' Similarly, Sufyan al-Thawri (may Allah have mercy on him) was asked about the children of polytheists, and he shouted at the questioner and said: 'Boy! Are you asking about that?' And my father, the scholar and mufti Muhammad Shafi (may Allah have mercy on him), when asked about such matters, would write in response the hadith of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him): 'Part of the perfection of one's Islam is leaving what does not concern him.' Then I found that one of the senior students of Imam Malik did the same with a king. He is Ziyad ibn Abd al-Rahman al-Qurtubi, known as Shabatun. Qadi Iyad (may Allah have mercy on him) narrated from him as follows: Habib said: We were sitting with Ziyad when a letter from a king came to him. He moistened his pen, wrote in it, then sealed the letter and sent it with the messenger. Ziyad said: Do you know what the author of this letter asked? He asked about the two pans of the scale of deeds on the Day of Judgment: are they made of gold or silver? So I wrote to him: Malik narrated to us from Ibn Shihab who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Part of the perfection of one's Islam is leaving what does not concern him, and you will come to know.'
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents several historical anecdotes illustrating the proper conduct of a mufti when faced with speculative or useless questions. The common theme is that a mufti should avoid engaging in theological speculation about matters that are not directly relevant to the questioner's practical life. The hadith 'Min husni islami al-mar'i tarkuhu ma la ya'nihi' (Part of the perfection of one's Islam is leaving what does not concern him) is invoked as a principle. The stories show that even great scholars rebuked such questions, and some answered only with this hadith as a gentle reminder. The lesson for a mufti is to discourage laypeople from asking about metaphysical details that have no bearing on their actions, and instead focus on actionable issues.

Key Terms
ما لا يعنيه (ma la ya'nihi): That which does not concern a person; in this context, matters that are irrelevant or speculative.
أغلوطات (aghlutat): Difficult questions meant to trip up the mufti; tricky or problematic questions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What hadith did Mufti Muhammad Shafi use to answer speculative questions?
A) 'Actions are judged by intentions'
B) 'Part of the perfection of one's Islam is leaving what does not concern him'
C) 'Seeking knowledge is obligatory upon every Muslim'
D) 'The best of you are those who learn the Quran and teach it'

Key Takeaway
A mufti should avoid answering speculative questions about unseen matters, instead guiding the questioner to what concerns them.
