O Allah, grant me success, guide me, keep me steady, combine for me correctness and reward, and protect me from error and deprivation. Amen. If he does not do that with every fatwa, he should do it at the first fatwa he gives that day for what he will give throughout the rest of his day, adding to it recitation of al-Fatiha, Ayat al-Kursi, and whatever is easy, for whoever persists in that is worthy to be successful in his fatwas.' And Ibn al-Qayyim, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'It is fitting for the mufti to frequently supplicate with the authentic hadith: O Allah, Lord of Jibril, Mika'il, and Israfil, Originator of the heavens and the earth, Knower of the unseen and the witnessed, You judge between Your servants concerning what they used to differ about. Guide me to what has been differed about by Your permission, indeed You guide whom You will to a straight path.' And our shaykh (meaning the eminent scholar Ibn Taymiyya, may Allah have mercy on him) used to supplicate often with that. And when matters were problematic for him, he would say: 'O Teacher of Ibrahim, teach me,' and he would frequently seek help with that, following the example of Mu'adh ibn Jabal, may Allah be pleased with him, when he said to Malik ibn Yukhamir al-Saksaki at his death, and he saw him crying, he said: 'By Allah, I do not cry for a worldly matter that I sought from you, but I cry for the knowledge and faith that I used to learn from you.' So Mu'adh ibn Jabal, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'Indeed, knowledge and faith are in their place; whoever seeks them finds them. Seek knowledge from four: from 'Uwaymir Abu al-Darda,' from 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud,' from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, and he mentioned the fourth. If you cannot get it from these, then the rest of the people of the earth are even more incapable, so you must rely on the Teacher of Ibrahim, peace be upon him.' And it is narrated from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, may Allah have mercy on him, that he would hardly give a fatwa or say anything without saying: 'O Allah, keep me safe and save me from myself.' -2- It is befitting for the mufti not to rush to answer in a gathering where there is someone more knowledgeable than him, but rather he should refer the question to him. An exception is when that more knowledgeable person commands him to answer, then he answers according to his knowledge. Ibn Nujaym, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'Among its conditions are maintaining order and justice among those seeking fatwas; he should not incline toward the wealthy, the associates of the sultan, or the emirs, but he should write the answer of the first who came, whether rich or poor.' -- It is befitting for the mufti not to answer except after attaining tranquility regarding the correctness of the answer, and he should not answer if there is any doubt in his heart, even if slight, and he should not be affected by the insistence of the seeker of fatwa to hasten the answer. Upon this is based the statement of those who said that it is not permissible to give fatwa while walking. It is narrated that Ibn Salam, may Allah have mercy on him, would sometimes have the seeker of fatwa insist on haste, mentioning that he came from a distant place, and he would say to him:
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is from a Hanafi work on Usul al-Iftaa, detailing the etiquettes and methodology of the mufti. It presents three main points. First, the mufti is encouraged to recite specific supplications before issuing a fatwa, seeking divine guidance and protection from error. The recommended supplications include verses from the Quran, such as 'Rabbi ishrah li sadri' and 'Subhanaka la ilma lana', as well as the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) about guidance in matters of disagreement. The passage also narrates that Shaykh Ibn Taymiyya would say 'Ya mu'allima Ibrahima 'allimni' when faced with difficult issues. Second, the mufti should not rush to answer if there is someone more knowledgeable present; he should defer to them unless instructed otherwise. Ibn Nujaym adds that the mufti must maintain justice and order, answering questions in the order they arrive without favoritism. Third, the mufti must not answer until he is fully confident in the correctness of his answer, and should not be swayed by the seeker's insistence. This is the basis for the rule that fatwa should not be given while walking, as it indicates haste. The example of Ibn Salam illustrates patience even when the seeker has traveled far.

Key Terms
الطُّمأنينة (al-ṭuma'nīna): The state of being fully satisfied and certain about the correctness of the answer, free from doubt.
مفتى (muftī): A qualified jurist who issues fatwas (legal responses) to questions.
إفتاء (iftā'): The act of issuing a fatwa, a non-binding legal opinion on a matter of Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Delay the answer until the scholar leaves.

Key Takeaway
The mufti must approach every fatwa with supplication, humility, and certainty, never rushing or relying solely on his own knowledge.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti
beginner
6 min
Mufti's Deference to a More Learned Scholar
Ruling on mufti's conduct in presence of a more learned scholar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a mufti's conduct when a more learned scholar is present and identify the exception.

Core Idea
A mufti must defer to a more learned scholar present, unless that scholar instructs him otherwise.

This lesson draws from the practice of the early scholars, such as Mu'adh ibn Jabal's emphasis on seeking knowledge from the best, and Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab's caution in giving fatwa. The core ruling is a key adab for muftis: when faced with a question in the presence of a more knowledgeable scholar, the mufti must not rush to answer; instead, he should refer the questioner to that scholar, unless the senior scholar explicitly delegates the answer to him.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
Recognize the scenario: a mufti is present in a gathering where a legal question is asked, and there is another scholar present who is more knowledgeable (a'lam).
This is a common situation in fatwa councils.
2
State the default ruling
The mufti must not (لا يبادِر) rush to answer. Instead, he should refer the question to the more learned scholar.
The Arabic term 'ينبغي' indicates a strong obligation (wajib) in the context of adab al-mufti.
3
Identify the exception
If the more learned scholar commands the mufti to answer, then the mufti may answer according to his knowledge.
The exception preserves the senior scholar's authority while allowing delegation.
4
Understand the rationale
This rule ensures that the most qualified person issues the fatwa, minimizing error and upholding the dignity of knowledge.
It reflects the principle that the fatwa is a serious responsibility.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Consider a junior mufti in a lecture where a senior scholar is present. A question is asked. The junior mufti knows the answer. What should he do?
He should remain silent and allow the senior to answer, unless instructed otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقال معاذبن جبل رضى الله تعالى عنه: إنّ العلم والإيمان مكانهما، من ابْتَغاهما وَجَدهما، أطلب العلم عند أربعة عند عويمر أبي الدرداء، وعند عبدالله بن مسعود، و أبي موسى الأشعري، وذكر الرابع. فإن عجز عنه هؤلاء، فسائر أهل الأرض عنه أعجز فعَليْكَ بمُعلّم إبراهيم صلوات الله عليه. وروى عن سعيد بن المُسَيَّب رحمه الله تعالى أنه كان لا يكاد يُفتى فتيا ولا يقول شيئاً إلا قال: 'اللهم سلمنى وسلّم مِنّى'. -٢- ينبغي للمفتى أن لا يُبادِرَ بالجواب في مجلس يوجد فيه من هو أعلم منه، بل يحوِّلُ السُّؤال إليه. ويُستثنى منه ما إذا أمره ذلك الرجل الأعلمُ بالجواب فحينئذٍ يُجيب حسب علمه.
Mu'adh ibn Jabal, may Allah be pleased with him, said: Indeed knowledge and faith are in their places; whoever seeks them will find them. Seek knowledge from four: from 'Uwaymir Abu al-Darda', from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, and he mentioned the fourth. If you cannot get them, then the rest of the people are more incapable, so stick to the teacher of Ibrahim, peace be upon him. And it is narrated from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, may Allah have mercy on him, that he would hardly ever give a fatwa or say anything without saying: 'O Allah, keep me safe and keep me safe from me.' [Rule 2] It is incumbent upon the mufti not to rush to answer in a gathering where there is someone more learned than him; rather, he should refer the question to him. An exception is made when that more learned man commands him to answer; then he answers according to his knowledge.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage begins with two narrations emphasizing the gravity of giving fatwa: the first from Mu'adh ibn Jabal, indicating that knowledge is to be sought from the best sources, and the second from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab showing his caution. The main ruling (point 2) is that a mufti should not answer a question in the presence of a more knowledgeable scholar; he must refer the questioner to that scholar. The exception is when the senior scholar instructs him to answer. This is a key adab of mufti, ensuring that authority is respected and that the most qualified person gives the ruling. The underlying principle is that the fatwa is a responsible position, and avoiding error requires deference to greater knowledge.

Key Terms
مفتى (mufti): The person issuing fatwas, a qualified jurist.
أعلم (a'lam): More knowledgeable in Islamic law.
يُبادِرُ (yubadir): To rush or hasten to answer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What should a mufti do if there is a more learned scholar present when a question is asked?
A) Give the answer himself to save time.
B) Refer the question to the more learned scholar.
C) Give the answer but only if the questioner insists.
D) Remain silent and let the questioner decide.

Key Takeaway
A mufti must defer to a more learned scholar present, unless that scholar instructs him otherwise.

Maintaining Order and Justice Between Questioners
Condition of maintaining order and fairness in answering

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of maintaining order and justice between questioners and apply it to identify violations in given scenarios.

Core Idea
A mufti must answer in order of arrival, favoring none – justice demands equality before the fatwa.

This passage from Ibn Nujaym (d. 970 AH) adds a crucial condition for the mufti: he must uphold order and fairness. The temptation to favor the powerful is real, but the mufti's duty is to treat all seekers equally, answering the first regardless of status. This principle safeguards the integrity of the fatwa and the mufti's character.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The passage begins 'ومِن شرائطها حفظه الترتيب والعدل بين المستفتين' – among the conditions (of fatwa) is maintaining order and justice between questioners.
This is a condition of proper fatwa practice, not a ruling on validity.
2
Recognize the prohibited behavior
The mufti must not lean toward the rich, rulers, or their aides: 'لا يميل إلى الأغنياء وأعوان السلطان والأمراء'.
Leaning means giving preferential treatment in answering.
3
State the required practice
He must write the answer for the first questioner, regardless of wealth or poverty: 'بل يكتب جواب السابق غنياً كان أو فقيراً'.
Order of arrival is the sole criterion.
4
Understand the underlying principle
This ensures fairness (العدل) and prevents corruption (ميل) in fatwa-giving, protecting the mufti's integrity.
The condition is moral and practical, not a legal nullifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن نجيم رحمه الله تعالى: 'ومِن شرائطها حفظه الترتيب والعدل بين المستفتين، لا يميل إلى الأغنياء وأعوان السلطان والأمراء، بل يكتب جواب السابق غنياً كان أو فقيراً.'
Ibn Nujaym, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'Among its conditions is his preservation of order and justice between the questioners; he should not lean towards the rich and the aides of the ruler and the princes; rather, he should write the answer of the first one, whether rich or poor.'
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
Ibn Nujaym states another condition for the mufti: he must maintain order and fairness among those seeking fatwa. He should not favor the wealthy or those connected to authority. Instead, he should answer in the order of arrival, regardless of social status. This ensures justice and prevents corruption. The term 'شرائطها' refers to the conditions of the fatwa or the office of mufti.

Key Terms
شرائط (shara'it): Conditions required for the validity or proper conduct of fatwa.
الترتيب (al-tartib): Order, i.e., answering in sequence of arrival.
العدل (al-'adl): Justice, fairness in treatment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) He should give them special consideration if they are rulers.

Key Takeaway
A mufti must answer in order of arrival, showing no favoritism to wealth or power.

Certainty Before Fatwa: Resisting Pressure
Ruling on requiring certainty before answering and resisting pressure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for giving fatwa with certainty and apply the rule that a mufti must not answer under doubt or pressure.

Core Idea
A mufti must not answer until certain; doubt or pressure cannot override the duty to be sure.

A mufti carries a heavy responsibility: every fatwa is a statement about Allah's law. This passage lays down a fundamental adab: do not answer unless you are fully certain, and do not let the questioner's urgency push you into haste. Through the examples of Ibn Salam and Sahnun, we learn that patience and thoroughness are better than a quick, uncertain answer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
Identify the mufti's requirement: he must not answer until he has attained tranquility (tuma'nina) about the correctness of the answer. Even a slight doubt (shubha) disqualifies the response.
This is the key condition: certainty is required.
2
Recognize the prohibition against pressure
The mufti must not be affected by the questioner's insistence (ilhaah) for a quick answer. External pressure does not change the obligation to be certain.
The questioner's urgency is irrelevant to the mufti's duty.
3
Analyze the illustrative stories
Examine how Ibn Salam and Sahnun handled insistence. Ibn Salam used a poetic rebuke to remind the questioner that he did not cause the journey; Sahnun took three days to research a difficult case (nazila), telling the questioner to be patient or go elsewhere.
Both scholars prioritized certainty over speed.
4
Apply the ruling to a modern scenario
If a questioner emails with urgency, the mufti should politely ask for time to research, following the adab of not being pressured. The mufti may explain the need for certainty and the gravity of the task.
Modern communication does not change the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينبغي للمفتى أن لا يُجيب إلا بعد حصولِ الطُّمأنينة على صحة الجواب، ولا يُجيب إن كان فى قلبه شُبهة ولو يسيرة، ولا يتأثر في ذلك بإلحاح المستفتى على التعجيل في الجواب. وعلى هذا يُحمل قول من قال إنه لا يجوز الإفتاء ماشياً. ويُروى أن ابن سلام رحمه الله تعالي ربّما كان المستفتى يُلح عليه بالتعجيل، ويذكر أنّه أتاه من مكان بعيد، فكان يقول له : فَلا نحن نادَيْناكَ من حيثُ جئتَنا ولا نحنُ عَمَّيْنا عليك المذاهبا. وروى عن سحنون أنّ رجلاً أتاه من صَطَفُوْرة، فسأل عن مسألة، فتردد إليه ثلاثة أيام فقال له: "أصلحك الله مسألتي في ثلاثة أيام." فقال له: "وما أصنع لك؟ ما حيلتى فى مسألتك؟ نازلةٌ مُعْضَلة، وفيها أقاويل، وأنا أتخيّر في ذلك. " فقال الرجل الصَّطفُورِى "وأنت أصلحك الله لكل معضلة!" فقال: "هَيْهَات ليس يا ابن أخى! بقولك أبذل لك لحمى ودمى إلى النار. ما أكثر مالا أعرف إن صبرت رجوت أن تنقلب بمسألتك، وإن أردت غيرى فامض، تُجاب عن ساعة " فقال: "إنما جئت إليك ولا أبتغى غيرك. قال: "فاصبر، عافاك الله. " ثم أجابه بعد ذلك.
The mufti should not answer except after attaining tranquility regarding the correctness of the answer, and he should not answer if there is any doubt in his heart, even if slight. He should not be affected by the insistence of the questioner for a quick answer. This is the interpretation of the saying that it is not permissible to give fatwa while walking. And it is narrated that Ibn Salam, may Allah have mercy on him, sometimes the questioner would insist on him for a quick answer, mentioning that he came from a far place, so he would say to him: 'We did not call you from where you came, nor did we make the schools obscure to you.' And it is narrated from Sahnun that a man came to him from Satfura, asked about a matter, and kept coming back for three days. He said: 'May Allah set you right, my question is three days long.' Sahnun replied: 'What can I do for you? What is my trick for your question? It is a difficult case, and there are opinions, and I am choosing.' The man from Satfura said: 'You, may Allah set you right, are for every difficult case!' Sahnun said: 'Far from it, O my nephew! By your saying, I would sacrifice my flesh and blood to the fire. There are many things I do not know. If you are patient, I hope that your question will be resolved. If you want someone else, go, you will be answered in an hour.' The man said: 'I only came to you, and I seek none other.' He said: 'Then be patient, may Allah cure you.' Then he answered him after that.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage emphasizes that a mufti must not give a fatwa until he is fully certain of its correctness. Even a slight doubt disqualifies the answer. He must not be swayed by the questioner's urgency, even if the questioner traveled far. Two stories illustrate this: Ibn Salam's poetic rebuke to a pressing questioner, and Sahnun's cautious approach over three days. The principle is that the responsibility of giving a fatwa is grave; it is better to delay than to err. The saying 'no fatwa while walking' means one should not give an answer in haste or without due deliberation.

Key Terms
طمأنينة (tuma'nina): Tranquility, certainty about the correctness of the answer.
شبهة (shubha): Doubt, even slight.
إلحاح (ilhaah): Insistence, pressure from the questioner.
نازلة (nazila): A difficult case or calamity requiring a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should a mufti do if he has even slight doubt about the answer?
A) Answer anyway to not keep the questioner waiting.
B) Refuse to answer until he has certainty.
C) Answer but mention the doubt.
D) Ask another mufti to answer.

Key Takeaway
Certainty is the foundation of valid fatwa; no pressure or urgency justifies a doubtful answer.

When a Mufti Must Refrain from Fatwa
Ruling on prohibition of fatwa when angry, scared, etc.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the specific states that impair a mufti's judgment and state the ruling that it is wajib to refrain.

Core Idea
If a mufti's mind is clouded by emotion or physical need, he must not issue a fatwa until clarity returns.

A mufti's fatwa is a legal opinion that can affect lives. Therefore, the mufti must be in a state of sound reasoning. This passage from Uṣūl al-Iftā' enumerates various external and internal factors that can impair judgment, and it sets a clear rule: when these factors overcome the mind, giving fatwa becomes obligatory to withhold.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the ruling
Identify that the text states it is wajib (obligatory) to refrain when the emotional state overcomes sound thinking.
The word 'wajaba' indicates obligation, not mere recommendation.
2
List the impairing states
Go through the text and note all conditions: anger, fear, lust, intense sadness, intense joy, sleepiness, hunger, severe illness, extreme heat or cold, and need to relieve oneself.
Both emotional and physical states are included.
3
Understand the underlying cause (illa)
The common factor is that these states 'overcome sound thinking' (ghalaba infi'ālahu 'alā ṣiḥḥati tafkīrihi). The illa is the impairment of sound reasoning.
The ruling is tied to the presence of this illa.
4
Apply the rule
If any of these states reaches a level where it dominates reasoning, the mufti must delay the fatwa until he returns to his normal state.
The prohibition is temporary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
-٤ ومما ينبغي للمفتى مراعاته أن لا يُفتي حال اشتغال قلبه بغضب أو رَهْبَةٍ أو شهوةٍ ممّا يُخرجه عن الاعتدال، وكذلك شدة الحزن وشدة الفرح ونحوه فإن غلب انفعاله على صحة تفكيره، وجَبَ عليه أن يكف عن الإفتاء إلى أن يعود إلى طبيعته، وكذلك إن كان به نُعاس أو جوع أو مرض شديد أو حرّ مزعج أو برد مؤلم أو مدافعة الأخبثين.
[Rule 4] Among what the mufti must observe is that he should not give fatwa while his heart is occupied with anger, or fear, or desire, which takes him out of moderation, and likewise intense sadness and intense joy and the like. For if his emotional state overcomes his sound thinking, it is obligatory upon him to refrain from giving fatwa until he returns to his normal state. Likewise if he is sleepy, hungry, severely ill, disturbed by heat, painful cold, or needing to relieve himself (from urine or stool).
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage outlines physical and emotional states that impair a mufti's judgment. The underlying cause (illa) is that such states prevent sound reasoning. If the impairment is significant, it becomes obligatory (wajib) to withhold the fatwa. This is based on the principle that fatwa is a serious responsibility requiring clarity of mind. The list includes anger, fear, lust, sadness, joy, sleepiness, hunger, illness, extreme temperature, and the need to relieve oneself. The mufti must wait until he is composed.

Key Terms
غضب (ghadab): Anger.
رهبة (rahba): Fear, terror.
شهوة (shahwa): Lust, strong desire.
انفعال (infi'al): Emotional state or agitation.
مدافعة الأخبثين (mudafa'at al-akhbathayn): Repelling urine and stool (need to relieve oneself).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He may give fatwa if he is confident.
B) It is obligatory to refrain until he returns to normal.
C) He should consult another mufti.
D) He can give fatwa but with caution.

Key Takeaway
A mufti must withhold fatwa when his judgment is impaired by emotion or physical need; this is a wajib adab.

Patience with Harsh Questioners: A Mufti's Adab
Ruling on patience with harsh questioners, with Quranic proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on patience with harsh questioners and identify the Quranic evidence for it.

Core Idea
A mufti must bear harshness patiently, following Prophet Dawud's example.

This lesson covers an important aspect of the mufti's conduct: how to handle harshness from those seeking fatwa. The scholars have derived this ruling from a Quranic example, emphasizing that patience is required as much as possible. The story of Prophet Dawud serves as the key evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the opening statement: 'ينبغي للمفتى أن يصبر على جفوة المستفتين مهما أمكن' – what is the ruling? It is an obligation (or strong recommendation) to be patient.
The phrase 'ينبغي' in this context indicates a wujub (obligation) in the mufti's adab.
2
Locate the evidence
The passage mentions that scholars use the story of Prophet Dawud (Surah Sad 38:21-24) as proof. Understand how the story demonstrates patience.
The two disputants came to Dawud abruptly, yet he listened and judged justly.
3
Connect the evidence to the ruling
Explain the wajh al-dalalah: the story shows that even a prophet endured sudden intrusion with patience; thus a mufti should follow this example.
A mufti is a successor of the prophets in conveying Allah's rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
-٥- ينبغي للمفتى أن يصبر على جَفْوَةِ المستفتين مهما أمكن. وقد استدل عليه العلماء بقصة داود عليه السلام في القرآن الكريم إذ جاءه المتخاصمان
[Rule 5] It is incumbent upon the mufti to be patient with the harshness of the questioners as much as possible. The scholars have used as evidence for this the story of Dawud (David) peace be upon him in the Quran, when the two disputants came to him.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This lesson teaches that the mufti must endure any rudeness or harshness from those seeking fatwa. This is derived from the story of Prophet Dawud in Surah Saad, where two disputants came to him abruptly. The mufti should follow this prophetic example. The term 'جفوة' means harshness or roughness. The mufti should not respond in kind.

Key Terms
جفوة (jafwa): Harshness, roughness, or discourtesy from the questioner.
صبر (sabr): Patience, forbearance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Caution and Deliberation in Giving Fatwa
Stories and admonition on avoiding haste in fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the adab of tathabbut and tahayyub from the practice of the Salaf and apply this caution to modern fatwa scenarios.

Core Idea
A mufti's careful deliberation is a form of worship that protects the religion.

This lesson examines a powerful story from Imam Sahnun that illustrates the ethical imperative for a mufti to avoid haste. The early scholars (Salaf) were deeply cautious about issuing fatwas, recognizing the immense responsibility of speaking on behalf of Allah. This narrative serves as a timeless reminder for every mufti to prioritize truth over convenience, even under pressure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the adab principle
Read the story and pinpoint the two key principles: tathabbut (thoroughness) and tahayyub (fear/awe). Understand how Sahnun's actions embody these.
Tathabbut involves verification; tahayyub involves humility before Allah.
2
Analyze Sahnun's reasoning
Why did Sahnun take three days? Consider his statement: 'I sacrifice my flesh and blood to the fire.' This shows he feared giving a wrong fatwa and being accountable. Also note his willingness to let the questioner go elsewhere.
Sahnun's reasoning is based on prioritizing the correctness of the ruling over personal convenience.
3
Extract the lesson for modern mufti
Apply the story to today: A mufti should not rush even if the questioner pressures. Delay for research and deliberation is praiseworthy, not a sign of incompetence.
This lesson is not about a specific ruling but about the ethical posture of the mufti.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَلا نحن نادَيْناكَ من حيثُ جئتَنا
ولا نحنُ عَمَّيْنا عليك المذاهبا
(1)
وروى عن سحنون أنّ رجلاً أتاه من صَطَفُوْرة، فسأل عن مسألة، فتردد إليه ثلاثة أيام فقال له: "أصلحك الله مسألتي في ثلاثة أيام." فقال له: "وما أصنع لك؟ ما حيلتى فى مسألتك؟ نازلةٌ مُعْضَلة، وفيها أقاويل، وأنا أتخيّر في ذلك." فقال الرجل الصَّطفُورِى "وأنت أصلحك الله لكل معضلة!" فقال: "هَيْهَات ليس يا ابن أخى! بقولك أبذل لك لحمى ودمى إلى النار. ما أكثر مالا أعرف إن صبرت رجوت أن تنقلب بمسألتك، وإن أردت غيرى فامض، تُجاب عن ساعة" فقال: "إنما جئت إليك ولا أبتغى غيرك." قال: "فاصبر، عافاك الله." ثم أجابه بعد ذلك. (۲) وقد ذكرنا عمل السلف في التثبت والتهيب من الفتيا ما فيه كفاية للانزجار عن التسرع في أمر الفتوى.
For we did not call you from where you came, nor did we blind the schools to you. (1) And it was narrated from Sahnun that a man came to him from Satfura, asking about a question, and he kept returning to him for three days. He said: "May God improve you, my question is three days old." He replied: "What can I do for you? What is my stratagem for your question? A difficult case, with various opinions, and I am deliberating over it." The man from Satfura said: "And you, may God improve you, are for every difficult case!" He said: "Far from it, O son of my brother! By your saying I sacrifice my flesh and blood to the fire. Most of what I do not know—if you are patient, I hope your question will be resolved; if you want someone else, go, you will get an answer in an hour." He said: "I only came to you and seek no other." He said: "Then be patient, may God forgive you." Then he answered him afterwards. (2) And we have mentioned the practice of the Salaf in being thorough and cautious about fatwa, which is sufficient to deter haste in the matter of fatwa.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is a mufti_adab section, using a story of Imam Sahnun to illustrate the extreme caution of early scholars. The poetry line emphasizes that the mufti does not blindly follow one school. Sahnun's three-day deliberation shows that a mufti must not rush even when pressed. His willingness to let the questioner seek another shows ethical prioritizing of truth over convenience. The concluding sentence reinforces that such caution is required to avoid giving fatwa hastily.

Key Terms
التثبت (al-tathabbut): Thoroughness and verification before issuing a ruling.
التهيب (al-tahayyub): Fear and awe of the responsibility of giving fatwa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many days did Sahnun take to answer the questioner?
A) One day
B) Two days
C) Three days
D) Four days

Key Takeaway
A mufti must exercise thoroughness and fear of accountability, even if it means delaying a fatwa.

Patience with Questioners: The Example of Dawud

Core Idea
The mufti must tolerate rudeness, following Dawood's example of patience with litigants.

This lesson draws on the story of Prophet Dawood (peace be upon him) to teach the etiquettes of patience for a mufti. The scholars derived evidence from the Quran that a mufti should endure harshness from questioners, as Dawood did not rebuke the litigants who entered abruptly. Al-Alusi's commentary reinforces this lesson, warning against pride and reminding the mufti of his own lowliness compared to the prophets.

How do scholars prove this principle? They use the Quranic story of Dawood (AS) in Surah Sad, where two litigants entered abruptly and said 'Do not be unjust' — Dawood did not rebuke them.

Structuring the Fatwa Answer: Ruling First, Evidence Second
Instructions on structuring a fatwa response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of fatwa composition by stating the ruling in clear language for laymen and deferring evidence, except when the questioner is a scholar.

Core Idea
The mufti's answer should begin with a clear ruling; evidence follows only for scholars.

This passage from Usul al-Ifta teaches the etiquette of writing a fatwa: the mufti must adapt his style to the questioner. For the common person, a simple ruling suffices; for the scholar, evidence may come first. This ensures the answer is immediately useful and avoids confusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the audience
Determine if the mustafti (questioner) is a scholar or a layman. This decision governs the entire structure of the fatwa.
The text distinguishes between ‘alim (scholar) and ‘ammi (layman).
2
Select the order of presentation
For a layman, state the ruling first in clear terms; defer evidence to later if at all. For a scholar, you may begin with evidence if appropriate.
The phrase 'فلا بأس بالابتداء بالدلائل' indicates permissibility, not obligation.
3
Choose the language level
Use simple, accessible language for all, unless the questioner is a scholar, wherein technical terminology is acceptable.
The wording 'عبارة سهلة' vs 'عبارة علمية اصطلاحية'.

Source Text

This is a mufti_adab section on the style of written fatwa. (7) The language should be simple for all, but scholarly jargon is acceptable for scholars. The final paragraph notes disagreement among jurists about whether to include evidence at all; some say only the ruling is needed. The text ends mid-sentence on this page.

Key Terms

مستفتى (mustafti): The person seeking a fatwa, the questioner.

The Mufti's Patience: Emulating Prophet Dawud
Exhortation for the mufti to emulate Dawud's patience with litigants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the prophetic example of patience for the mufti and apply it to a situation involving a harsh litigant.

Core Idea
A mufti must bear harshness from litigants, following Dawud's forbearance.

This lesson draws from the Quranic story of Prophet Dawud (peace be upon him) to illustrate the patience a mufti must have when dealing with disputants. The passage highlights that even when litigants are abrupt or seemingly disrespectful, the mufti should not react harshly. Instead, he should emulate Dawud's calmness and recognize his own humble status before Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Prophetic Example
Recall the story of Prophet Dawud (AS) as narrated in the passage: two litigants entered abruptly and said 'Be not unjust.' Note that Dawud did not rebuke them.
This is from Surah Sad, verse 22.
2
Understand the Lesson
Recognize that the author, citing Al-Alusi, explains that Dawud's tolerance indicates that a judge (or mufti) should bear similar treatment, especially when the litigant is in the right.
3

Contrast this with the common tendency of muftis to become angry at slight words. The author emphasizes that the mufti's status is negligible compared to the prophet, so pride is misplaced.
4
Reflect on Personal Status
Internalize the lesson that a mufti is a servant of the Shari'ah and should not be quick to anger. The passage ends with a prayer for good morals.

Conciseness and Elaboration in Fatwa Writing
Sets norms for fatwa length and style.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of conciseness in fatwa composition and identify when elaboration is required per al-Qarafi's exception.

Core Idea
The mufti’s fatwa should be concise but not harmful; significant public-interest matters call for elaboration.

A fatwa must be clear and focused. The default rule is to limit the answer to the ruling and evidence, avoiding emotion and unnecessary words. However, al-Qarafi highlights an exception: when the case involves major religious or public interests, the mufti should elaborate to clarify truth and deter wrongdoing. This lesson teaches you to distinguish between conciseness and harmful brevity, and to know when to expand.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule
Read the first part of the passage: the fatwa should be limited to stating the legal ruling and its evidence, free from emotional language and filler. This is the baseline.
The phrase 'الاقتصار على الجواب' (limiting to the answer) is key.
2
Define harmful brevity and tiresome verbosity
The passage warns against 'الإيجاز المُخِل' (harmful brevity) which omits essential details, and 'الإطناب المُمل' (tiresome verbosity) which adds no benefit. Understand these as the pitfalls to avoid.
Brevity is good unless it harms the completeness of the answer.
3
Recognize the exception
Al-Qarafi's statement introduces the exception: for a 'واقعة عظيمة' (great incident) related to important religious matters or public interests, elaboration is not only allowed but recommended.
The exception includes situations connected to governance (ولاق الأمور).
4
Analyze the conditions for elaboration
The exception applies when the case is significant and requires clarifying truth, terrifying criminals, urging good, and repelling evil. The mufti should use clear expressions and strong language.
This is not a blanket permission; the mufti must judge the importance.
5
Apply the rule to scenarios
Practice differentiating: a routine question (e.g., zakat calculation) should be concise; a question about a new financial instrument affecting the community may require elaboration.
The mufti's discretion is guided by the potential impact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
-۸- ينبغي أن تكون الفتوى مقتصرةً علي بيان الحكم الشرعي ودليله الفقهي، خالياً عن العاطفية وبواعث المدح والغضب الوقتي، كما ينبغى أن تكونَ عبارة الفتوى خالية عن الإيجاز المُخِل أو الإطناب المُمل، و أن لا تكونَ كلمة من كلمةِ الجواب خاليةً عن فائدة جديدة، فيجتنب في الفتوى عن التمهيدات الطويلة، وبيان الأسرار والحكم، إلا إذا سئل عنها المستفتى، وعلم المفتى بأنّه يُفيد. لكن قال القرافي رحمه الله تعالى: "ومتى كان الاستفتاء في واقعة عظيمة تتعلق بمهام الدين أو مصالح المسلمين ولها تعلق بولاق الأمور فيَحْسُن من المفتى الإسهاب فى القول، وكثرة البيان والمبالغة في إيضاح الحق بالعبارات السريعة الفهم والتهويل على الجناة، والحض على المبادرة لتحصيل المصالح ودرء المفاسد ويحسن بسط القول في هذه المواطن وذكر الأدلة الحاثة على تلك المصالح الشرعية، وإظهار النكير في الفتيا على ملابس المنكرات المُجمع على تحريمها وقبحها. ولا ينبغى ذلك في غير هذه المواطن، بل الاقتصار على الجواب."
8. The fatwa should be limited to stating the legal ruling and its juristic evidence, free from emotionalism and triggers of praise and momentary anger. Likewise, the wording of the fatwa should be free from harmful brevity or tiresome verbosity, and not a single word of the answer should be devoid of new benefit. Therefore, long preambles and explanations of secrets and wisdom are to be avoided in the fatwa, unless the questioner asks about them and the mufti knows that it will be beneficial. However, al-Qarafi (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'When the request for a fatwa pertains to a great incident related to important matters of the religion or the interests of Muslims and is connected to the governance of affairs, then it is fitting for the mufti to elaborate in speech, to multiply explanation, to exaggerate in clarifying the truth with expressions that are quickly understood, to terrify the criminals, to urge promptness in attaining benefits and repelling harms, to expand speech in such situations, to mention the evidence that encourages those religious interests, and to show disapproval in the fatwa against those who engage in prohibited and detestable acts unanimously agreed upon. This should not be done in other situations; instead, the answer should be concise.'
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage lays down the etiquette of the fatwa composition. The mufti should stick to the legal ruling and its evidence, avoid emotional language, unnecessary preambles, and verbosity. The fatwa should be concise but not so brief that it omits essential points. An exception is made for significant issues affecting public interest: in such cases, elaboration is encouraged to clarify the truth and deter wrongdoing, as per al-Qarafi's advice. The mufti must judge the context and adjust the length and style accordingly.

Key Terms
الاقتصار على الجواب (al-iqtisar 'ala al-jawab): The principle of being concise in the fatwa answer, limiting it to the ruling and evidence without extraneous material.
الإيجاز المُخِل (al-ijaz al-mukhial): Brevity that omits essential details, rendering the fatwa harmful or incomplete.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always
B) When the questioner asks for details
C) For matters of great religious or public interest
D) Never

Key Takeaway
The mufti must balance conciseness with detail according to the significance of the case.

Restricting the Use of 'Haram' in Fatwa
Rule on mufti's terminology: restrict 'haram' to definitive prohibitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that the mufti must reserve the term 'haram' for matters proven by definitive evidence, and use alternative expressions for disputed issues. You will also be able to distinguish between 'haram', 'ghair ja'iz', and other levels of prohibition in Hanafi fatwa practice.

Core Idea
The word 'haram' carries absolute certainty; use it only for qat'i prohibitions.

In fatwa, language is not merely descriptive—it carries legal weight. Using the word 'haram' loosely can create undue religious burden or misrepresent the strength of evidence. This lesson presents a fundamental adab (etiquette) for the mufti: reserve 'haram' for what is proven by definitive (qat'i) evidence, and for matters open to ijtihad or lacking clear text, employ softer terms like 'ghair ja'iz' (not permissible) or 'ghair mardi' (not satisfactory) according to the degree of disapproval. This rule preserves the integrity of fatwa and avoids misleading the questioner.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core rule
The passage states that the mufti should not use 'haram' except for matters established by qat'i (definitive) proofs. Recognize this as a binding adab for issuing fatwas.
Qat'i proofs include explicit Quranic verses, mutawatir hadith, and ijma'.
2
Understand the rationale
Reflect on why 'haram' must be restricted: it implies absolute certainty. Using it for disputed matters overstates the evidence and may cause the questioner to treat a non-definitive issue as definitively forbidden.
This protects both the mufti's integrity and the questioner's religious practice.
3
Distinguish the alternative terms
For matters without definitive text or subject to ijtihad, use 'ghair ja'iz' (not permissible) or 'ghair mardi' (not satisfactory). Note that 'ghair ja'iz' is commonly used in Hanafi fatawa for actions that are strongly disliked or prohibited by weaker evidence, without labeling them as 'haram'.
The choice depends on the degree of disapproval (darajat al-nakir).
4
Apply the rule to examples
Consider two scenarios: (1) an action explicitly prohibited in the Quran with clear text (e.g., interest/riba) → 'haram'; (2) an action on which scholars differ (e.g., eating shellfish) → use 'ghair ja'iz' or mention the ikhtilaf without declaring it haram.
This application sharpens the mufti's linguistic precision.
5
Recognize the broader tradition
The passage cites Imam Malik (incomplete quote) indicating that early scholars were cautious in declaring things haram. This reinforces the Hanafi stance that 'haram' is a weighty term and should not be applied to every disliked or probable prohibition.
This adab is part of the larger Hanafi tradition of cautious fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۹ - وينبغي للمفتى أن لا يُطلق لفظ "الحرام" إلا على ما ثبتت حرمته بدلائل قطعيّة. أمّا فى الأمور التي ليس فيها نص، أو فى الأمور المجتهد فيها، فيُعرض عن هذا التعبير إلى تعبير آخر، مثل قوله: غير جائز، أو غير مرضي، حسب درجات النكير. وقال الإمام مالك رحمه الله تعالى: "لم يكن من أمر الناس
9. The mufti should not use the word 'haram' except for what has been established as prohibited by definitive evidence. As for matters in which there is no text, or matters of ijtihad, he should avoid this expression and use another expression, such as 'not permissible' or 'not satisfactory', according to the degrees of disapproval. And Imam Malik (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'It was not from the practice of the people...'
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This is an important caution for muftis: the term 'haram' carries a strong weight and implies certainty. It should only be applied when the prohibition is established by qat'i (definitive) proofs, such as explicit Quranic text or mutawatir hadith. For matters that are subject to ijtihad or have no clear text, softer terms like 'ghair ja'iz' (not permissible) or 'ghair mardi' (not satisfactory) should be used, reflecting the degree of disapproval. The incomplete quote from Imam Malik traditionally emphasizes that early practice was cautious in declaring things haram.

Key Terms
حرام (haram): Legally prohibited with certainty, requiring definitive evidence.
غير جائز (ghair ja'iz): Not permissible; a milder term used for actions not proven haram by definitive evidence.
قطعي (qat'i): Definitive, certain; used for both thubut (transmission) and dalalah (meaning) of textual evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should a mufti use instead of 'haram' for matters of ijtihad?
A) 'Wajib'
B) 'Haram' is still acceptable
C) 'Ghair ja'iz' or 'ghair mardi'
D) 'Makruh' is the only option

Key Takeaway
The mufti must restrict the word 'haram' to matters proven by definitive evidence; for disputed or less certain matters, he should use milder terms like 'ghair ja'iz'.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti
intermediate
8 min
The Mufti's Language and Scope in Fatwa
Guidelines on fatwa wording and use of 'haram'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the guidelines on fatwa composition to evaluate whether a given fatwa is appropriately worded and within the mufti's scope, and identify when to use alternative expressions to 'haram'.

Core Idea
A fatwa must be evidence-based, concise, and avoid emotional language; the term 'haram' is reserved for matters proven by definitive texts.

This passage from Usul al-Iftaa lays down crucial adab for the mufti when composing a fatwa. It emphasizes that the fatwa should be confined to the legal ruling and its evidence, free from emotionalism and excessive digression. However, it also permits elaboration in cases of great public importance. Additionally, the mufti must exercise caution in using the word 'haram', restricting it to matters with definitive proofs, and using softer expressions for ijtihadi issues.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle of fatwa composition
The opening paragraph warns that evidence should be stated in a scholarly manner to avoid misunderstanding by the lay public.
This sets the tone: the mufti must consider his audience.
2
Analyze rule 8: fatwa content and wording
The fatwa should be limited to the ruling and its evidence, devoid of emotionalism, and free from verbosity or conciseness that lacks benefit.
Al-Qarafi's exception allows elaboration in matters of major religious or public interest.
3
Examine rule 9: proper use of 'haram'
The mufti must not use 'haram' except for matters proven by definitive evidences (qat'i). For ijtihadi matters, alternative expressions like 'ghayr ja'iz' or 'ghayr mardi' are to be used.
Imam Malik's quote emphasizes the gravity of declaring halal/haram without certainty.
4
Distinguish between ordinary and exceptional fatwas
Ordinary fatwas should be concise; exceptional fatwas (major interests) may be elaborate and emotionally impactful.
This distinction prevents misuse of the exception.
5
Apply the guidelines to a hypothetical fatwa
Evaluate a sample fatwa: does it meet the criteria of being evidence-based, devoid of unwarranted language, and correctly using 'haram'?
This step solidifies understanding through practical application.

أحكام الإفتاء و منهجه
وإن ذكر المفتى دليل الحكم على هذا الأساس، فليكن بعبارةٍ علمية رصينة لا يفهمها إلا العلماء، لأن العامة قد يُسيئون فهم الأدلة، فيقعون في اشتباه. -۸- ينبغي أن تكون الفتوى مقتصرةً علي بيان الحكم الشرعي ودليله الفقهي، خالياً عن العاطفية وبواعث المدح والغضب الوقتي، كما ينبغى أن تكونَ عبارة الفتوى خالية عن الإيجاز المُخِل أو الإطناب المُمل، و أن لا تكونَ كلمة من كلمةِ الجواب خاليةً عن فائدة جديدة، فيجتنب في الفتوى عن التمهيدات الطويلة، وبيان الأسرار والحكم، إلا إذا سئل عنها المستفتى، وعلم المفتى بأنّه يُفيد. لكن قال القرافي رحمه الله تعالى: "ومتى كان الاستفتاء في واقعة عظيمة تتعلق بمهام الدين أو مصالح المسلمين ولها تعلق بولاق الأمور فيَحْسُن من المفتى الإسهاب فى القول، وكثرة البيان والمبالغة في إيضاح الحق بالعبارات السريعة الفهم والتهويل على الجناة، والحض على المبادرة لتحصيل المصالح ودرء المفاسد ويحسن بسط القول في هذه المواطن وذكر الأدلة الحاثة على تلك المصالح الشرعية، وإظهار النكير في الفتيا على ملابس المنكرات المُجمع على تحريمها وقبحها. ولا ينبغى ذلك في غير هذه المواطن، بل الاقتصار على الجواب. ۹ - وينبغي للمفتى أن لا يُطلق لفظ "الحرام" إلا على ما ثبتت حرمته بدلائل قطعيّة. أمّا فى الأمور التي ليس فيها نص، أو فى الأمور المجتهد فيها، فيُعرض عن هذا التعبير إلى تعبير آخر، مثل قوله: غير جائز، أو غير مرضي، حسب درجات النكير. وقال الإمام مالك رحمه الله تعالى: "لم يكن من أمر الناس ولا مَن مضى، ولا مِنْ سَلَفِنا الذين يُقتدى بهم، ويُعوّل الإسلامُ عليهم أن يقولوا هذا حلال وهذا حرام، ولكن يقول: أنا أكْرَهُ كذا، وأُحِبُّ كذا. وأما حلال وحرام، فهذا الافتراء على الله، أما سمعت قول الله تعالى: قُلْ أَرَيْتُم مَّا أَنزَلَ اللهُ لَكُم مِّن رِزْقٍ فَجَعَلْتُم مِّنْهُ حَرَامًا وَحَلَلًا قُلْ وَاللَّهُ أَذِنَ لَكُمْ أَمْ عَلَى اللَّهِ تَفْتَرُونَ ﴾ [يونس : ٥٩]"، لأن الحلال ما أحله الله، والحرام ما حرمه. -١٠- ينبغى للمفتى أن يُراعى التيسير على النّاس في ما تعارضت فيه الأدلة من الأمور التي تعُمّ بها البلوى. قال سفيانُ الثَّوري رحمه الله تعالى: " إنما العِلْمُ عندنا الرخصة من ثقة، فأما التشديد فيُحْسِنُه كل احد ) وفي جانب آخر، يجب على المفتى أن يَحْذَر من أن يجلبه التيسير في الأمور المنصوصة إلى الانسلال من ربقة التكليف. -١١- ينبغى للمفتى استشارة الفقهاء العابدين فى المسائل الجديدة التي ليس فيها نص صريح في الكتاب والسنة ولا في الفقه المتوارث والأصل في ذلك ما رواه سيدنا على بن أبي طالب رضى الله تعالى عنه قال: " قُلْتُ: يَا رَسُولَ اللهِ ا إِنْ نَزَلَ بِنَا أَمْرٌ لَيْسَ فِيْهِ بَيَانٌ، أَمْرٌ وَلَا نَهْى ، فَمَا تَأْمُرُنَا؟ " قَالَ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ : " شَاوِرُوْا الْفُقَهَاءَ العَابِدِيْنَ، وَلَا تُمْضُوْا فِيْهِ رَأْيَ خَاصَّةٍ .
Rulings of issuing fatwa and its methodology. And if the mufti mentions the evidence of the ruling on this basis, it should be in a solid scientific expression that only scholars understand, because the common people may misunderstand the evidence, falling into confusion. -8- It is necessary that the fatwa be limited to stating the legal ruling and its juristic evidence, devoid of emotionalism and motives of praise and momentary anger. Likewise, the wording of the fatwa should be free from deficient conciseness or tedious verbosity, and every word of the answer should not be devoid of new benefit. So lengthy introductions and mentioning secrets and wisdoms should be avoided in the fatwa, unless the questioner asks about them and the mufti knows that it is beneficial. However, al-Qarafi (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'When the inquiry is about a great incident related to the important matters of religion or the interests of Muslims, and it is connected to the conduct of affairs, then it is good for the mufti to elaborate in speech, increase explanation, exaggerate in clarifying the truth with expressions that are quick to understand, and terrify the criminals, and urge promptness in achieving benefits and repelling harms. It is good to elaborate speech in these situations and mention the evidences urging those legal interests, and to show disapproval in the fatwa against those who commit unanimously prohibited and ugly evils. This is not proper in other situations; rather, one should restrict to the answer.' 9 - It is necessary for the mufti not to use the word 'haram' except for what is proven to be forbidden by definitive evidences. As for matters in which there is no text, or matters of ijtihad, one should turn from this expression to another expression, such as saying 'not permissible' or 'not satisfactory', according to the degrees of disapproval. Imam Malik (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'It was not from the practice of the people, nor those who passed, nor from our predecessors who are followed and upon whom Islam relies, to say 'this is halal and this is haram.' Rather, they would say: 'I dislike such and such, and I like such and such.' As for 'halal' and 'haram', that is slander against Allah. Have you not heard the saying of Allah Almighty: Say, 'Have you seen what Allah has sent down to you of provision, and you have made some of it unlawful and some lawful?' Say, 'Has Allah permitted you, or do you invent lies about Allah?' [Yunus:59].' Because halal is what Allah made lawful, and haram is what He made unlawful. -10- It is necessary for the mufti to observe facilitation for the people in matters where evidences conflict, regarding general afflictions. Sufyan al-Thawri (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'Knowledge with us is the concession from a trustworthy person; as for strictness, anyone can do it well.' On the other hand, the mufti must beware that facilitation in matters that have texts should not lead him to slipping away from the yoke of obligation. -11- It is necessary for the mufti to consult the worshipping jurists in new issues in which there is no explicit text in the Qur'an and Sunnah, nor in the inherited fiqh. The basis for this is what was narrated by our master Ali ibn Abi Talib (may Allah be pleased with him) who said: 'I said: O Messenger of Allah, if there descends upon us a matter in which there is no clarification, no command and no prohibition, what do you command us?' He (peace be upon him) said: 'Consult the worshipping jurists, and do not follow the opinion of a few.'
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Iftaa presents key adab (rules of conduct) for the mufti in composing his fatwa. It is a methodological guide covering eight to eleven points. The text opens with a general point that if a mufti states the evidence, he must do so in a precise scholarly manner to avoid misinterpretation by the lay public. Rule eight emphasizes that the fatwa should be confined to the legal ruling and its evidence, avoiding emotional language and unnecessary digressions. Al-Qarafi is cited for an exception: in cases involving major religious or public interests, elaboration and emphasis are encouraged. Rule nine warns against using the term 'haram' indiscriminately; it must be reserved for matters proven by definitive evidence. Imam Malik's quote underscores the gravity of declaring something halal or haram without certainty. Rule ten balances facilitation (taysir) with caution: in matters of conflicting evidence and general need, leniency is preferred, but not in clearly textually defined obligations. Sufyan al-Thawri's saying supports preferring reliable concessions. Rule eleven mandates consultation with righteous scholars (fuqaha' 'abidin) on novel issues lacking clear texts, citing a hadith from Ali (RA). This principle safeguards against individual opinion and ensures communal scholarly input. The commentary follows the author's order, clarifying each guideline's rationale and limits.

Key Terms
الحرام (al-haram): That which is forbidden by definitive evidences (adillah qat'iyyah); not to be applied to matters of ijtihad or without clear text.
الفتوى (al-fatwa): A legal response stating the shari'ah ruling and its evidence, confined to that scope and avoiding emotional or excessive language.
المستفتى (al-mustafti): The one seeking a fatwa; the questioner whose needs (like asking for detailed wisdom) may affect the length of the response.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to rule 9, when should a mufti avoid using the word 'haram'?
A) When the ruling is based on clear Quranic text
B) When the matter involves general affliction (balaa)
C) When the matter lacks a definitive text or is subject to ijtihad
D) When consulting with other scholars

Key Takeaway
The mufti must confine his fatwa to the ruling and its evidence, avoid emotionalism, and reserve the term 'haram' for matters with definitive proofs.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti

Facilitation and Consultation in Fatwa
Guidelines on facilitation and consultation in fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principles of taysir (facilitation) and shura (consultation) to novel issues and distinguish when facilitation is appropriate versus when it risks undermining clear obligations.

Core Idea
In matters of general need with conflicting evidence, the mufti should lean toward facilitation, but never at the expense of clear texts; consultation is obligatory for novel issues.

This passage continues the rules of fatwa, addressing two key aspects: the mufti's attitude toward facilitation and the importance of consultation. The mufti is encouraged to grant concessions in matters where evidence conflicts and the need is widespread, but warned not to undermine clear obligations. Then, the mufti is directed to consult with righteous jurists for new issues lacking explicit textual guidance, based on a hadith from Ali (RA).

Guided Path
1
Identify rule 10: the principle of taysir
The mufti should facilitate for people in matters where evidences conflict and the problem is widespread. Sufyan al-Thawri remarks that concessions from trustworthy sources are true knowledge.
Taysir is preferred, but only in cases of conflicting evidence, not when texts are clear.
2
Understand the balance: facilitation vs. clear texts
The mufti must beware that facilitation does not lead to slipping away from obligations in matters that have clear scriptural texts.
This is a warning against using taysir to circumvent established rulings.
3
Analyze rule 11: the obligation of consultation
For new issues without explicit text in Quran, Sunnah, or inherited fiqh, the mufti must consult the 'fuqaha' abidin' (worshipping jurists).
This is based on a hadith instructing consultation and avoiding individual opinion.
4
Examine the hadith evidence
Ali (RA) asked the Prophet (SAW) about matters without clear guidance; the Prophet commanded consultation with the fuqaha' abidin and not to follow a selective opinion.
This establishes the authority for mandatory consultation in new cases.
5
Apply both rules to a modern scenario
Consider a fatwa on organ transplantation: no clear text, conflicting opinions. The mufti should consult scholars and, if evidence conflicts, lean toward facilitation for the general need.
This demonstrates the practical integration of both rules.

According to rule 10, what is the mufti warned against when applying facilitation?
A) Being too strict
B) Slipping from obligation in matters with clear texts
C) Ignoring the questioner's needs
D) Using emotional language

Key Takeaway
The mufti must balance facilitation with adherence to clear texts, and must consult righteous scholars for novel issues before issuing a fatwa.

The Salaf's Caution in Declaring Halal and Haram
Critique of labeling things halal/haram without divine authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the adab of avoiding definitive halal/haram declarations without divine text, based on the practice of the salaf and Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Declaring something halal or haram without divine authority is a form of iftira' (fabrication against Allah) – a major sin.

The mufti's role is to convey Allah's rulings, not to create them. This lesson examines the caution exercised by the righteous predecessors (salaf) when asked about matters. Instead of issuing absolute fatwas, they expressed personal preference, leaving the definitive judgment to Allah. Understanding this adab is crucial for responsible fatwa-giving.

Guided Path
1
Identify the adab rule
Recognize that the salaf avoided saying 'this is halal' or 'this is haram' about matters not clearly covered by texts. They considered such declarations as potential iftira' against Allah.
2
Understand the alternative expression
Instead of definitive rulings, the salaf used phrases like 'I like such and such' or 'I dislike such and such'. This shows a cautious approach, distinguishing personal opinion from divine law.
3
Analyze the Quranic evidence
The verse from Surah Yunus (10:59) is cited: it condemns those who declare things halal or haram without Allah's permission. This establishes that such pronouncements are acts of iftira'.
The verse uses the verb 'taftarun' (you fabricate lies), indicating the gravity of this sin.
4
Apply to mufti practice
A mufti must restrict his fatwa to clear textual evidence. When evidence is lacking, he should express caution, say 'I do not know', or state his personal inclination without attributing it to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا مَن مضى، ولا مِنْ سَلَفِنا الذين يُقتدى بهم، ويُعوّل الإسلامُ عليهم أن يقولوا هذا حلال وهذا حرام، ولكن يقول: أنا أكْرَهُ كذا، وأُحِبُّ كذا. وأما حلال وحرام، فهذا الافتراء على الله، أما سمعت قول الله تعالى: قُلْ أَرَيْتُم مَّا أَنزَلَ اللهُ لَكُم مِّن رِزْقٍ فَجَعَلْتُم مِّنْهُ حَرَامًا وَحَلَلًا قُلْ وَاللَّهُ أَذِنَ لَكُمْ أَمْ عَلَى اللَّهِ تَفْتَرُونَ ﴾ [يونس : ٥٩]، لأن الحلال ما أحله الله، والحرام ما حرمه.
And none of those who passed, nor any of our predecessors who are followed and upon whom Islam relies, used to say 'this is halal and this is haram.' Rather, they would say: 'I dislike such-and-such, and I like such-and-such.' As for halal and haram, that is fabricating lies against Allah. Have you not heard the saying of Allah Almighty: 'Say, have you considered what Allah has sent down to you of provision, and you have made some of it haram and some halal? Say, has Allah permitted you, or do you fabricate lies against Allah?' [Yunus: 59] Because halal is what Allah has made halal, and haram is what He has made haram.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage establishes a foundational adab for the mufti: extreme caution in labeling matters as halal or haram based on personal opinion. The author quotes the practice of the righteous predecessors (salaf) who, when asked about a matter, would express personal inclination (like or dislike) rather than issuing definitive rulings. To declare something halal or haram is to claim knowledge of Allah's law, which requires clear textual evidence. The Quranic verse (Yunus 59) is cited as a stern warning against such presumption. The lesson here is that a mufti must not speak on behalf of Allah without authority; his role is to convey Allah's rulings, not to innovate them.

Key Terms
حلال (halal): Legally permissible as determined by divine texts.
حرام (haram): Legally prohibited as determined by divine texts.
افتراء (iftira'): Fabricating lies against Allah by attributing rulings to Him without basis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'I do not know.'
B) 'I like this and I dislike that.'
C) 'It may be halal or haram.'
D) 'You should ask someone else.'

Key Takeaway
The mufti must never declare something halal or haram without clear divine evidence; instead, he may express personal preference.

Balancing Taysir and Caution in Fatwa
Directive to mufti on balancing ease and caution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two guiding principles for a mufti when balancing ease and caution, and identify the exception to taysir.

Core Idea
Taysir applies where evidence conflicts and need is widespread; taysir does not apply to explicit rulings.

A mufti must navigate between making fatwa accessible and preserving the integrity of the Shariah. This passage, attributed to the Hanafi tradition, provides a clear directive: ease is encouraged in areas of legitimate ijtihad, but caution is paramount when rulings are explicit. Understanding this balance is essential for responsible ifta.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first directive
The mufti is instructed to consider ease for people in matters where proofs conflict and the issue is widespread. This is the domain of taysir.
taysir is based on the maxim 'hardship brings ease'.
2
Recall the authority of rukhsah
Sufyan al-Thawri's quote emphasizes that true knowledge is conveying reliable concessions (rukhsah), not arbitrary strictness.
Strictness (tashdid) is easy but often unfounded.
3
Identify the second directive
The mufti must beware that taysir does not lead to slipping out of the yoke of obligation (ribqat al-taklif) in explicitly prescribed matters (mansusah).
mansusah are rulings from clear texts, not open to ijtihad.
4
Understand the distinction
The key is distinguishing between areas of ijtihad (where taysir is permissible) and areas of definitive texts (where taysir is prohibited).
This distinction prevents misuse of the taysir principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
-١٠- ينبغى للمفتى أن يُراعى التيسير على النّاس في ما تعارضت فيه الأدلة من الأمور التي تعُمّ بها البلوى. قال سفيانُ الثَّوري رحمه الله تعالى: إنما العِلْمُ عندنا الرخصة من ثقة، فأما التشديد فيُحْسِنُه كل احد. وفي جانب آخر، يجب على المفتى أن يَحْذَر من أن يجلبه التيسير في الأمور المنصوصة إلى الانسلال من ربقة التكليف.
10. It is befitting for the mufti to consider ease for people in matters where proofs conflict and which are of widespread need. Sufyan al-Thawri (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'Knowledge with us is only the concession (rukhsah) from a reliable source; as for strictness (tashdid), anyone can do that.' On the other hand, the mufti must beware that ease in explicitly prescribed matters does not lead to slipping out of the yoke of obligation.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage presents a balanced approach to fatwa-giving. The first part encourages the mufti to prefer concessions (rukhsah) in cases where evidence is conflicting and the matter is commonly encountered by people, to avoid hardship. The quote from Sufyan al-Thawri emphasizes that real knowledge lies in conveying reliable concessions, as strictness is easy but unqualified. However, the second part warns that this principle does not apply to explicit, well-established rulings (mansusah); using ease to evade clear obligations is prohibited. Thus, the mufti must distinguish between areas of ijtihad (where concessions are permissible) and areas of definitive texts (where no discretion is allowed).

Key Terms
تيسير (taysir): Facilitation; giving an easier fatwa based on valid rukhsah.
رخصة (rukhsah): A concessionary ruling due to necessity or hardship, as opposed to the original stricter rule (azimah).
ربقة التكليف (ribqat al-taklif): The yoke of obligation; the binding nature of sharia commands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, in which type of issues should the mufti consider ease?
A) All issues without exception.
B) Issues with explicit texts only.
C) Issues where evidence conflicts and the need is widespread.
D) Only issues related to worship.

Key Takeaway
Taysir is permissible only in areas of conflicting evidence and widespread need, not in explicit rulings.

Consultation with Pious Jurists for Novel Issues
Directive to consult pious jurists on new issues

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the prophetic command to consult pious jurists on novel issues and explain why consultation is a necessary adab for the mufti.

Core Idea
A mufti must consult pious jurists on novel matters; the Prophet ﷺ commanded collective deliberation.

This lesson focuses on a fundamental adab (etiquette) for the mufti: consultation. When faced with a new issue not covered by explicit texts, the mufti should not act alone. The author presents a key hadith from Ali (ra) and the consistent practice of the early caliphs, establishing that seeking counsel is not optional but an integral part of responsible ifta.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue Type
The passage discusses cases where 'there is no explicit text (nass sarih) in the Book and Sunnah, nor in inherited fiqh.' Recognize that these are novel issues (nawazil) requiring ijtihad.
Not every fatwa requires consultation; only those beyond clear texts.
2
Recall the Prophetic Command
Memorize the hadith: 'Consult the pious jurists, and do not pass a single opinion on it.' The imperative 'shawiru' (consult) and the prohibition 'la tumdu' (do not pass) show the strength of the directive.
The phrase 'ra'y khassah' means a single individual's opinion.
3
Define 'Fuqaha al-Abidin'
The jurists to be consulted must combine deep knowledge (fiqh) with piety (ibadah). This underscores that mere intellectual ability is insufficient; taqwa is essential.
Hanafi tradition: a mufti must be 'alim and 'abid.
4
Observe the Salaf Practice
The author cites that the Rightly Guided Caliphs and the Tabi'un routinely consulted and denounced solitary fatwas. The example of Umar gathering the people of Badr highlights the gravity.
Even senior companions did not issue fatwas alone on major issues.
5
Apply the Lesson to Modern Fatwa
Today, a mufti facing a new financial instrument or medical procedure should convene a council of qualified scholars rather than deciding alone.
This is the basis for fatwa councils and collective ijtihad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
-١١- ينبغى للمفتى استشارة الفقهاء العابدين فى المسائل الجديدة التي ليس فيها نص صريح في الكتاب والسنة ولا في الفقه المتوارث والأصل في ذلك ما رواه سيدنا على بن أبي طالب رضى الله تعالى عنه قال: قُلْتُ: يَا رَسُولَ اللهِ إِنْ نَزَلَ بِنَا أَمْرٌ لَيْسَ فِيْهِ بَيَانٌ، أَمْرٌ وَلَا نَهْى ، فَمَا تَأْمُرُنَا؟ قَالَ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ : شَاوِرُوْا الْفُقَهَاءَ العَابِدِيْنَ، وَلَا تُمْضُوْا فِيْهِ رَأْيَ خَاصَّةٍ. ولم تزل الاستشارة فى الأمور الفقهية دأب الخلفاء الراشدين رضى الله تعالى عنهم والسلف الصالحين، وقد أخرج الدارمي في سننه جملة من آثارهم في ذلك، حتى وقع الإنكار من بعض التابعين على من يستبد بالإفتاء وينفرد به دون أن يستشير غيره. وروى عن أبي حصين قال: إن أحدهم ليفتي في المسئلة، ولو وَرَدَت على عمر بن الخطاب لجمع لها أهل بدر.
11. It is befitting for the mufti to consult the pious jurists concerning new issues for which there is no explicit text in the Book and Sunnah, nor in inherited fiqh. The basis for this is what was narrated from our master Ali ibn Abi Talib (may Allah be pleased with him) who said: 'I said, O Messenger of Allah, if there occurs to us a matter for which there is no clear command or prohibition, what do you order us?' He (peace be upon him) said: 'Consult the pious jurists, and do not pass a single opinion on it.' Consultation in juristic matters has always been the practice of the Rightly Guided Caliphs (may Allah be pleased with them) and the righteous predecessors. Al-Darimi narrated in his Sunan a number of their reports about that, to the point that some of the Tabi'in denounced those who gave fatwa independently without consulting others. And it is narrated from Abu Husayn that he said: 'One of them gives a fatwa on a matter, whereas if it had come to Umar ibn al-Khattab, he would have gathered for it the people of Badr.'
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage emphasizes the importance of consultation (shura) in issuing fatwas, especially for novel issues not directly addressed in the primary sources. The hadith from Ali (ra) provides the prophetic sanction: consult the 'fuqaha al-abidin' (jurists who are also devout worshippers). The phrase 'la tumdu fihi ra'y khassah' (do not pass a single opinion) indicates that no individual should decide alone; collective deliberation is required. The author supports this with the practice of the early caliphs and the criticism of solitary fatwas. The quote from Abu Husayn underscores the gravity: even a matter that a minor mufti might answer quickly would have led Umar to convene the elite scholars of Badr (indicating high-level consultation). This teaches that the mufti must be humble and seek input from qualified peers, not act arrogantly.

Key Terms
فقهاء العابدين (fuqaha al-abidin): Jurists who combine deep knowledge of fiqh with devout worship and piety.
نص صريح (nass sarih): An explicit and unambiguous text from Quran or Sunnah directly addressing the issue.
استشارة (istisharah): Consultation; seeking advice from others before giving a fatwa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What did the Prophet (pbuh) instruct when asked about a matter with no clear ruling?
A) 'Make ijtihad on your own.'
B) 'Consult the pious jurists and do not pass a single opinion.'
C) 'Follow the opinion of the caliph.'
D) 'Wait for revelation.'

Key Takeaway
A mufti must consult pious jurists on novel issues not covered by explicit texts, as commanded by the Prophet ﷺ and practiced by the Rightly Guided Caliphs.

Avoiding Deviant Fatwas: Adhering to the Majority
Warning against irregular fatwas contradicting the majority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the basis for avoiding shadhdh fatwas and identify the consequence of contradicting the majority opinion.

Core Idea
The ummah cannot agree on error – a mufti must adhere to the mainstream position.

This lesson addresses a crucial adab for the mufti: the obligation to avoid issuing fatwas that deviate from the consensus of the majority of scholars. The foundation is a powerful hadith affirming that the Muslim community will not unite upon error. Understanding this principle protects both the mufti and the community from following isolated or weak opinions that could lead to misguidance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Prohibition
The passage warns against 'fatawa shadhdhah' – irregular fatwas that contradict the majority (jamahir) of the nation's jurists. Note the term 'shadhdh' implies an anomalous opinion that lacks strong support.
2
Examine the Primary Evidence
The hadith from Abdullah ibn Umar (ra) states that Allah does not unite the ummah upon error, that Allah's hand is with the congregation, and that deviating from the congregation leads to the Fire. This establishes the sanctity of the majority view.
The second hadith, though cut off, typically concludes with the instruction to follow the 'great majority' (al-sawad al-a'zam).
3
Apply the Principle to Fatwa
The mufti must weigh the strength of opinions. The default should be the position held by the majority (jumhur) of the madhhab. A shadhdh opinion may only be followed if it has overwhelming evidence, which is rare in Hanafi practice.
This is part of the mufti's hierarchy of authorities: zahir al-riwayah > nawadir > fatawa mutakhkhirin.
4
Recognize the Consequence
The hadith warns that deviation from the congregation leads to the Fire, illustrating the spiritual danger of disregarding the majority. This underscores the responsibility of the mufti to safeguard the community.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
-١٢- التجنّب عن الفتاوى الشاذة التى تُخالِفُ جماهير فقهاء الأمّة. رَوى عبد الله بن عمر رضى الله تعالى عنهما عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: إنَّ اللهَ لا يَجْمَعُ أُمَّنِي -أو قال: أُمَّةً مُحَمَّدٍ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ- عَلَى ضَلَالَةٍ، وَيَدُ اللهِ عَلَى الجَمَاعَةِ، وَمَنْ شَدَّ شَدَّ إِلى النَّارِ. ورُوى عن أنس بن مالك رضى الله عنه عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: إنَّ أُمَّتِي لا تَجْتَمِعُ عَلَى ضَلَالَةٍ، فَإِذَا رَأَيْتُمْ اخْتِلَافا،
12. Avoidance of deviant fatwas that contradict the majority of the jurists of the nation. Abdullah ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with them both) narrated from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Indeed, Allah does not unite my ummah – or he said: the ummah of Muhammad (peace be upon him) – upon error, and the hand of Allah is with the congregation. And whoever deviates deviates towards the Fire.' And it is narrated from Anas ibn Malik (may Allah be pleased with him) from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Indeed, my ummah does not unite upon error, so when you see disagreement...'
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage issues a strong warning against issuing shadhdh (irregular, anomalous) fatwas that contradict the majority (jumhur) of scholars. The basis is the well-known hadith that the Muslim community will not agree on error. Therefore, the mufti must give weight to the majority opinion and avoid isolated or weak positions that could lead people astray. The second hadith, though cut off, typically continues with the instruction to follow the 'great majority' (al-sawad al-a'zam). This principle is especially important in fatwa because a deviant opinion, if followed, could mislead many. The mufti's duty is to protect the community by adhering to reliable, mainstream positions.

Key Terms
فتاوى شاذة (fatawa shadhdhah): Irregular or anomalous fatwas that deviate from the well-established majority view without strong evidence.
جماهير (jamahir): The majority of scholars; the prevailing opinion.
ضلالة (dalalah): Error; deviation from the truth in religious matters.
السواد الأعظم (al-sawad al-a'zam): The great majority; the mainstream body of scholars and the community.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of deviating from the congregation according to the hadith in this passage?
A) The person will be forgiven.
B) The person deviates towards the Fire.
C) The person is still considered a believer.
D) The person must seek repentance.

Key Takeaway
The hadith that the ummah will not unite upon error obliges the mufti to adhere to the majority opinion and avoid shadhdh fatwas, as deviation leads to the Fire.
